period</a></li>\n<li><a href="/wiki/History_of_the_United_Kingdom_during_the_First_World_War" title="History of the United Kingdom during the First World War">First World War</a></li>\n<li><a href="/wiki/Interwar_Britain" title="Interwar Britain">Interwar period</a></li>\n<li><a href="/wiki/History_of_the_United_Kingdom#Second_World_War_1939\xe2\x80\x931945" title="History of the United Kingdom">Second World War</a></li>\n<li><a href="/wiki/Political_history_of_the_United_Kingdom_(1945%E2%80%93present)" title="Political history of the United Kingdom (1945\xe2\x80\x93present)">Post-war period (political history)</a></li>\n<li><a href="/wiki/Social_history_of_the_United_Kingdom_(1945%E2%80%93present)" title="Social history of the United Kingdom (1945\xe2\x80\x93present)">Post-war period (social history)</a></li></ul></li>\n<li><a href="/wiki/History_of_the_Republic_of_Ireland" title="History of the Republic of Ireland">Late modern Ireland</a></li>\n<li><a href="/wiki/History_of_the_Isle_of_Man#Modern_period" title="History of the Isle of Man">Late modern Mann</a></li></ul></div></div></td>\n</tr><tr><td style="text-align:right;font-size:115%;padding-top: 0.6em;"><div class="plainlinks hlist navbar mini"><ul><li class="nv-view"><a href="/wiki/Template:History_of_the_British_Isles" title="Template:History of the British Isles"><abbr title="View this template">v</abbr></a></li><li class="nv-talk"><a href="/wiki/Template_talk:History_of_the_British_Isles" title="Template talk:History of the British Isles"><abbr title="Discuss this template">t</abbr></a></li><li class="nv-edit"><a class="external text" href="//en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Template:History_of_the_British_Isles&amp;action=edit"><abbr title="Edit this template">e</abbr></a></li></ul></div></td></tr></tbody></table>\n<p><b>England in the Late Middle Ages</b> concerns the <a href="/wiki/History_of_England" title="History of England">history of England</a> during the <a href="/wiki/Late_Middle_Ages" title="Late Middle Ages">late medieval period</a>, from the thirteenth century, the end of the <a href="/wiki/Angevin_kings_of_England" title="Angevin kings of England">Angevins</a>, and the accession of <a href="/wiki/Henry_III_of_England" title="Henry III of England">Henry III</a> \xe2\x80\x93 considered by many to mark the start of the <a href="/wiki/Plantagenet" class="mw-redirect" title="Plantagenet">Plantagenet</a> dynasty \xe2\x80\x93 until the accession to the throne of the <a href="/wiki/Tudor_dynasty" class="mw-redirect" title="Tudor dynasty">Tudor dynasty</a> in 1485, which is often taken as the most convenient marker for the end of the <a href="/wiki/Middle_Ages" title="Middle Ages">Middle Ages</a> and the start of the <a href="/wiki/English_Renaissance" title="English Renaissance">English Renaissance</a> and <a href="/wiki/Early_modern_Britain" title="Early modern Britain">early modern Britain</a>.\n</p><p>At the accession of <a href="/wiki/Henry_III_of_England" title="Henry III of England">Henry III</a> only a remnant of English holdings remained in <a href="/wiki/Gascony" title="Gascony">Gascony</a>, for which English kings had to pay <a href="/wiki/Homage_(feudal)" title="Homage (feudal)">homage</a> to the French, and the barons were in revolt. Royal authority was restored by his son who inherited the throne in 1272 as <a href="/wiki/Edward_I_of_England" title="Edward I of England">Edward I</a>. He reorganized his possessions, and gained control of Wales and most of Scotland. His son <a href="/wiki/Edward_II" class="mw-redirect" title="Edward II">Edward II</a> was defeated at the <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Bannockburn" title="Battle of Bannockburn">Battle of Bannockburn</a> in 1314 and lost control of Scotland. He was eventually deposed in a coup and from 1330 his son <a href="/wiki/Edward_III_of_England" title="Edward III of England">Edward III</a> took control of the kingdom. Disputes over the status of Gascony led Edward III to lay claim to the <a href="/wiki/Kingdom_of_France" title="Kingdom of France">French throne</a>, resulting in the <a href="/wiki/Hundred_Years%27_War" title="Hundred Years&#39; War">Hundred Years\' War</a>, in which the English enjoyed success, before a French resurgence during the reign of Edward III\'s grandson <a href="/wiki/Richard_II_of_England" title="Richard II of England">Richard II</a>.\n</p><p>The fourteenth century saw the <a href="/wiki/Great_Famine_of_1315%E2%80%931317" class="mw-redirect" title="Great Famine of 1315\xe2\x80\x931317">Great Famine</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Black_Death" title="Black Death">Black Death</a>, catastrophic events that killed around half of England\'s population, throwing the economy into chaos and undermining the old political order. With a shortage of farm labour, much of England\'s arable land was converted to pasture, mainly for sheep. Social unrest followed in the <a href="/wiki/Peasants%27_Revolt" title="Peasants&#39; Revolt">Peasants\' Revolt</a> of 1381.\n</p><p>Richard was deposed by <a href="/wiki/Henry_of_Bolingbroke" class="mw-redirect" title="Henry of Bolingbroke">Henry of Bolingbroke</a> in 1399, who as Henry IV founded the <a href="/wiki/House_of_Lancaster" title="House of Lancaster">House of Lancaster</a> and reopened the war with France. His son <a href="/wiki/Henry_V_of_England" title="Henry V of England">Henry V</a> won a decisive victory at <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Agincourt" title="Battle of Agincourt">Agincourt</a> in 1415, reconquered Normandy and ensured that his infant son <a href="/wiki/Henry_VI_of_England" title="Henry VI of England">Henry VI</a> would inherit both English and French crowns after his unexpected death in 1421. However, the French enjoyed another resurgence and by 1453 the English had lost almost all their French holdings. Henry VI proved a weak king and was eventually deposed in the <a href="/wiki/Wars_of_the_Roses" title="Wars of the Roses">Wars of the Roses</a>, with <a href="/wiki/Edward_IV_of_England" title="Edward IV of England">Edward IV</a> taking the throne as the first ruling member of the <a href="/wiki/House_of_York" title="House of York">House of York</a>. After his death and the taking of the throne by his brother as <a href="/wiki/Richard_III" class="mw-redirect" title="Richard III">Richard III</a>, an invasion led by <a href="/wiki/Henry_VII_of_England" title="Henry VII of England">Henry Tudor</a> and his victory in 1485 at the <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Bosworth_Field" title="Battle of Bosworth Field">Battle of Bosworth Field</a>, marked the end of the Plantagenet dynasty.\n</p><p>English government went through periods of reform and decay, with parliament emerging as an important part of the administration. Women had an important economic role and noblewomen exercised power on their estates in their husbands\' absence. The English began to see themselves as superior to their neighbours in the British Isles and regional identities continued to be significant. New reformed monastic orders and preaching orders reached England from the twelfth century, pilgrimage became highly popular and <a href="/wiki/Lollardy" title="Lollardy">Lollardy</a> emerged as a major heresy from the later fourteenth century. The <a href="/wiki/Little_Ice_Age" title="Little Ice Age">Little Ice Age</a> had a significant impact on agriculture and living conditions. Economic growth began to falter at the end of the thirteenth century, owing to a combination of overpopulation, land shortages and depleted soils. Technology and science was driven in part by the Greek and Islamic thinking that reached England from the twelfth century. In warfare, mercenaries were increasingly employed and adequate supplies of ready casequipped and uniformed. Medieval England produced art in the form of paintings, carvings, books, fabrics and many functional but beautiful h became essential for the success of campaigns. By the time of Edward III, armies were smaller, but the troops were better objects. Literature was produced in Latin and French and from the reign of Richard II there was an upsurge in the use of <a href="/wiki/Middle_English" title="Middle English">Middle English</a> in poetry. Music and singing were important were used in religious ceremonies, court occasions and to accompany theatrical works. During the twelfth century the Anglo-Norman architectural style became more ornate, with pointed arches derived from France, termed <a href="/wiki/Early_English_Gothic" class="mw-redirect" title="Early English Gothic">Early English Gothic</a>.\n</p>\n<div class="toclimit-3"><div id="toc" class="toc"><input type="checkbox" role="button" id="toctogglecheckbox" class="toctogglecheckbox" class="toctext">External links</span></a></li>\n</ul>\n</div>\n</div>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Political_history">Political history</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=England_in_the_Late_Middle_Ages&amp;action=edit&amp;section=1" title="Edit section: Political history">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Eleonora_Jindra2.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/88/Eleonora_Jindra2.jpg/220px-Eleonora_Jindra2.jpg" width="220" height="176" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/88/Eleonora_Jindra2.jpg/330px-Eleonora_Jindra2.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/8/88/Eleonora_Jindra2.jpg 2x" data-file-width="386" data-file-height="308" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Eleonora_Jindra2.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Twelfth-century depiction of <a href="/wiki/Henry_II_of_England" title="Henry II of England">Henry II</a> and <a href="/wiki/Eleanor_of_Aquitaine" title="Eleanor of Aquitaine">Eleanor of Aquitaine</a> holding court</div></div></div>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="House_of_Plantagenet">House of Plantagenet</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=England_in_the_Late_Middle_Ages&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: House of Plantagenet">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/House_of_Plantagenet" title="House of Plantagenet">House of Plantagenet</a></div>\n<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Background">Background</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=England_in_the_Late_Middle_Ages&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: Background">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>\n<p><a href="/wiki/Geoffrey_Plantagenet,_Count_of_Anjou" title="Geoffrey Plantagenet, Count of Anjou">Geoffrey Plantagenet, Count of Anjou</a>\'s marriage to the <a href="/wiki/Empress_Matilda" title="Empress Matilda">Empress Matilda</a> meant that he gained control of England and Normandy by 1154, and the marriage of Geoffrey\'s son <a href="/wiki/Henry_II_of_England" title="Henry II of England">Henry Curtmantle</a>, to <a href="/wiki/Eleanor_of_Aquitaine" title="Eleanor of Aquitaine">Eleanor of Aquitaine</a> expanded the family\'s holdings into what was later termed the <a href="/wiki/Angevin_Empire" title="Angevin Empire">Angevin Empire</a>. As Henry II he consolidated his holdings and acquired nominal control of Wales and Ireland. His son <a href="/wiki/Richard_I_of_England" title="Richard I of England">Richard I</a> was a largely absentee English king, concerned more with the crusades and his holdings in France. His brother <a href="/wiki/John_of_England" class="mw-redirect" title="John of England">John</a>\'s defeats in France weakened his position in England. The rebellion of his English vassals resulted in the treaty called <a href="/wiki/Magna_Carta" title="Magna Carta">Magna Carta</a>, which limited royal power and established <a href="/wiki/Common_law" title="Common law">common law</a>. This would form the basis of every constitutional battle through the thirteenth and fourteenth centuries.<sup id="cite_ref-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-1">&#91;1&#93;</a></sup> However, both the barons and the crown failed to abide by the terms of Magna Carta, leading to the <a href="/wiki/First_Barons%27_War" title="First Barons&#39; War">First Barons\' War</a> in which the rebel barons invited an invasion by <a href="/wiki/Louis_VIII_of_France" title="Louis VIII of France">Prince Louis</a>. John\'s death and <a href="/wiki/William_Marshal,_1st_Earl_of_Pembroke" title="William Marshal, 1st Earl of Pembroke">William Marshall</a>\'s appointment as the protector of the nine-year-old <a href="/wiki/Henry_III_of_England" title="Henry III of England">Henry III</a> are considered by some historians to mark the end of the Angevin period and the beginning of the Plantagenet dynasty.<sup id="cite_ref-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-2">&#91;2&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h4><span id="Henry_III_.281216.E2.80.9372.29"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Henry_III_(1216\xe2\x80\x9372)">Henry III (1216\xe2\x80\x9372)</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=England_in_the_Late_Middle_Ages&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: Henry III (1216\xe2\x80\x9372)">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Henry_III_of_England" title="Henry III of England">Henry III of England</a></div>\n<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:WLA_vanda_Cast_of_Tomb_Effigy_Henry_III.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/09/WLA_vanda_Cast_of_Tomb_Effigy_Henry_III.jpg/220px-WLA_vanda_Cast_of_Tomb_Effigy_Henry_III.jpg" width="220" height="142" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/09/WLA_vanda_Cast_of_Tomb_Effigy_Henry_III.jpg/330px-WLA_vanda_Cast_of_Tomb_Effigy_Henry_III.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/09/WLA_vanda_Cast_of_Tomb_Effigy_Henry_III.jpg/440px-WLA_vanda_Cast_of_Tomb_Effigy_Henry_III.jpg 2x" data-file-width="4147" data-file-height="2674" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:WLA_vanda_Cast_of_Tomb_Effigy_Henry_III.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Cast of the effigy of <a href="/wiki/Henry_III_of_England" title="Henry III of England">Henry III</a> in Westminster Abbey, c. 1272</div></div></div>\n<p>When Henry III came to the throne in 1216, much of his holdings on the continent were occupied by the French and many of the barons were in rebellion as part of the <a href="/wiki/First_Barons%27_War" title="First Barons&#39; War">First Barons\' War</a>. Marshall won the war with victories at the battles of <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Lincoln_(1217)" title="Battle of Lincoln (1217)">Lincoln</a> and <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Dover_(1217)" class="mw-redirect" title="Battle of Dover (1217)">Dover</a> in 1217, leading to the <a href="/wiki/Treaty_of_Lambeth" title="Treaty of Lambeth">Treaty of Lambeth</a> by which Louis renounced his claims.<sup id="cite_ref-Jones221_3-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Jones221-3">&#91;3&#93;</a></sup> In victory, the Marshal Protectorate reissued the <i>Magna Carta</i> agreement as a basis for future government.<sup id="cite_ref-DanzigerGillinghamp271_4-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-DanzigerGillinghamp271-4">&#91;4&#93;</a></sup> Despite the treaty hostilities continued and Henry was forced to make significant constitutional concessions to the newly crowned Louis VIII of France and Henry\'s stepfather <a href="/wiki/Hugh_X_of_Lusignan" title="Hugh X of Lusignan">Hugh X of Lusignan</a>. Between them, they overran much of the remnants of Henry\'s continental holdings, further eroding the Plantagenet grip on the continent. Henry saw such similarities between himself and England\'s then patron saint <a href="/wiki/Edward_the_Confessor" title="Edward the Confessor">Edward the Confessor</a> in his struggle with his nobles<sup id="cite_ref-Jones234_5-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Jones234-5">&#91;5&#93;</a></sup> that he gave his first son the Anglo-Saxon name Edward and built the saint a magnificent, still-extant shrine at <a href="/wiki/Westminster" title="Westminster">Westminster</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Schama2000p172_6-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Schama2000p172-6">&#91;6&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>The barons were resistant to the cost in men and money required to support a war to restore Plantagenet holdings on the continent. In order to motivate his barons, Henry III reissued Magna Carta and the <a href="/wiki/Charter_of_the_Forest" title="Charter of the Forest">Charter of the Forest</a> in return for a tax that raised the huge sum of \xc2\xa345,000. This was enacted in an assembly of the barons, bishops and magnates that created a compact in which the feudal prerogatives of the king were debated and discussed in the political community.<sup id="cite_ref-Jones2012p227_7-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Jones2012p227-7">&#91;7&#93;</a></sup> Henry was forced to agree to the <a href="/wiki/Provisions_of_Oxford" title="Provisions of Oxford">Provisions of Oxford</a> by barons led by his brother-in-law, <a href="/wiki/Simon_de_Montfort,_6th_Earl_of_Leicester" title="Simon de Montfort, 6th Earl of Leicester">Simon de Montfort</a>, under which his debts were paid in exchange for substantial reforms. He was also forced to agree to the <a href="/wiki/Treaty_of_Paris_(1259)" title="Treaty of Paris (1259)">Treaty of Paris</a> with <a href="/wiki/Louis_IX_of_France" title="Louis IX of France">Louis IX of France</a>, acknowledging the loss of the <a href="/wiki/Dukedom_of_Normandy" class="mw-redirect" title="Dukedom of Normandy">Dukedom of Normandy</a>, Maine, Anjou and Poitou, but retaining the <a href="/wiki/Channel_Islands" title="Channel Islands">Channel Islands</a>. The treaty held that "islands (if any) which the king of England should hold", he would retain "as peer of France and Duke of Aquitaine".<sup id="cite_ref-UnitedNations_8-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-UnitedNations-8">&#91;8&#93;</a></sup> In exchange Louis withdrew his support for English rebels, ceded three bishoprics and cities, and was to pay an annual rent for possession of <a href="/wiki/Agenais" title="Agenais">Agenais</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Rothwell1975_9-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Rothwell1975-9">&#91;9&#93;</a></sup> Disagreements about the meaning of the treaty began as soon as it was signed.<sup id="cite_ref-Lauterpacht_10-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Lauterpacht-10">&#91;10&#93;</a></sup> The agreement resulted in English kings having to pay homage to the French monarch, thus remaining French vassals, but only on French soil. This was one of the indirect causes of the <a href="/wiki/Hundred_Years_War" class="mw-redirect" title="Hundred Years War">Hundred Years War</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-11" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-11">&#91;11&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h5><span class="mw-headline" id="Second_Barons_War_and_the_establishment_of_Parliament">Second Barons War and the establishment of Parliament</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=England_in_the_Late_Middle_Ages&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: Second Barons War and the establishment of Parliament">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h5>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Second_Barons%27_War" title="Second Barons&#39; War">Second Barons\' War</a></div>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:332px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Montfort_Evesham.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/64/Montfort_Evesham.jpg/330px-Montfort_Evesham.jpg" width="330" height="97" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/64/Montfort_Evesham.jpg/495px-Montfort_Evesham.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/6/64/Montfort_Evesham.jpg 2x" data-file-width="500" data-file-height="147" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Montfort_Evesham.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Death and mutilation of <a href="/wiki/Simon_de_Montfort,_6th_Earl_of_Leicester" title="Simon de Montfort, 6th Earl of Leicester">Simon de Montfort</a> at the <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Evesham" title="Battle of Evesham">Battle of Evesham</a></div></div></div>\n<p>Friction intensified between the barons and the king. Henry repudiated the Provisions of Oxford and obtained a <a href="/wiki/Papal_bull" title="Papal bull">papal bull</a> in 1261 exempting him from his oath. Both sides began to raise armies. <a href="/wiki/Edward_I_of_England" title="Edward I of England">Prince Edward</a>, Henry\'s eldest son, was tempted to side with his godfather Simon de Montfort, and supported holding a Parliament in his father\'s absence, before he decided to support his father. The barons, under Montfort, captured most of south-eastern England. The war, its prelude and aftermath, included a wave of violence targeting Jewish communities in order to destroy evidence of the Baron\'s debts, a particular grievance of the rebels. 500 Jews died in London, and communities were decimated in <a href="/wiki/Worcester" title="Worcester">Worcester</a>, <a href="/wiki/Canterbury" title="Canterbury">Canterbury</a> and elsewhere.<sup id="cite_ref-12" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-12">&#91;12&#93;</a></sup> At the <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Lewes" title="Battle of Lewes">Battle of Lewes</a> in 1264, Henry and Edward were defeated and taken prisoner. Montfort summoned the <a href="/wiki/Montfort%27s_Parliament" class="mw-redirect" title="Montfort&#39;s Parliament">Great Parliament</a>, regarded as the first English Parliament worthy of the name because it was the first time cities and burghs sent representatives.<sup id="cite_ref-Schama2000p181_13-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Schama2000p181-13">&#91;13&#93;</a></sup> Edward escaped and raised an army. He defeated and killed Montfort at the <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Evesham" title="Battle of Evesham">Battle of Evesham</a> in 1265.<sup id="cite_ref-14" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-14">&#91;14&#93;</a></sup> Savage retribution was exacted on the rebels and authority was restored to Henry. Edward, having pacified the realm, left England to join Louis IX on the <a href="/wiki/Ninth_Crusade" title="Ninth Crusade">Ninth Crusade</a>, funded by an unprecedented levy of one-twentieth of every citizen\'s movable goods and possessions. He was one of the last crusaders in the tradition aiming to recover the Holy Lands. Louis died before Edward\'s arrival and the result was anticlimactic; Edward\'s small force limited him to the relief of <a href="/wiki/Acre,_Israel" title="Acre, Israel">Acre</a> and a handful of raids. Surviving a murder attempt by an <a href="/wiki/Assassins" title="Assassins">assassin</a>, Edward left for Sicily later in the year, never to return on crusade. The stability of England\'s political structure was demonstrated when Henry III died and his son succeeded as Edward I; the barons swore allegiance to Edward even though he did not return for two years.<sup id="cite_ref-Jones2012p293_15-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Jones2012p293-15">&#91;15&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h4><span id="Edward_I_.281272.E2.80.931307.29"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Edward_I_(1272\xe2\x80\x931307)">Edward I (1272\xe2\x80\x931307)</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=England_in_the_Late_Middle_Ages&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: Edward I (1272\xe2\x80\x931307)">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Edward_I_of_England" title="Edward I of England">Edward I of England</a></div>\n<h5><span class="mw-headline" id="Conquest_of_Wales">Conquest of Wales</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=England_in_the_Late_Middle_Ages&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7" title="Edit section: Conquest of Wales">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h5>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Conquest_of_Wales_by_Edward_I" class="mw-redirect" title="Conquest of Wales by Edward I">Conquest of Wales by Edward I</a></div>\n<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:North_Wales_Caernarfon_Castle.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/80/North_Wales_Caernarfon_Castle.jpg/220px-North_Wales_Caernarfon_Castle.jpg" width="220" height="144" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/80/North_Wales_Caernarfon_Castle.jpg/330px-North_Wales_Caernarfon_Castle.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/80/North_Wales_Caernarfon_Castle.jpg/440px-North_Wales_Caernarfon_Castle.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1280" data-file-height="838" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:North_Wales_Caernarfon_Castle.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Caernarfon Castle, the "capital" of English rule in North Wales for two centuries after the conquest.<sup id="cite_ref-16" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-16">&#91;16&#93;</a></sup></div></div></div>\n<p>From the beginning of his reign Edward I sought to organise his inherited territories. As a devotee of the cult of <a href="/wiki/King_Arthur" title="King Arthur">King Arthur</a> he also attempted to enforce claims to primacy within the British Isles. Wales consisted of a number of <a href="/wiki/Principality" title="Principality">princedoms</a>, often in conflict with each other. <a href="/wiki/Llywelyn_ap_Gruffudd" title="Llywelyn ap Gruffudd">Llywelyn ap Gruffudd</a> held north Wales in fee to the English king under the Treaty of Woodstock, but had taken advantage of the English civil wars to consolidate his position as Prince of Wales and maintained that his principality was \'entirely separate from the rights\' of England. Edward considered Llywelyn \'a rebel and disturber of the peace\'. Edward\'s determination, military experience and skillful use of ships ended Welsh independence by driving Llywelyn into the mountains. Llywelyn later died in battle. The <a href="/wiki/Statute_of_Rhuddlan" title="Statute of Rhuddlan">Statute of Rhuddlan</a> extended the shire system, bringing Wales into the English legal framework. When Edward\'s son was born he was proclaimed as the first English <a href="/wiki/Prince_of_Wales" title="Prince of Wales">Prince of Wales</a>. Edward\'s Welsh campaign produced one of the largest armies ever assembled by an English king in a formidable combination of heavy Anglo-Norman cavalry and Welsh archers that laid the foundations of later military victories in France. Edward spent around \xc2\xa3173,000 on his two Welsh campaigns, largely on a network of castles to secure his control.<sup id="cite_ref-Jones2012p314_17-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Jones2012p314-17">&#91;17&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h5><span class="mw-headline" id="Domestic_policy">Domestic policy</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=England_in_the_Late_Middle_Ages&amp;action=edit&amp;section=8" title="Edit section: Domestic policy">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h5>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Edward_I_of_England" title="Edward I of England">Edward I of England</a></div>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Gal_nations_edward_i.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b2/Gal_nations_edward_i.jpg/170px-Gal_nations_edward_i.jpg" width="170" height="270" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b2/Gal_nations_edward_i.jpg/255px-Gal_nations_edward_i.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b2/Gal_nations_edward_i.jpg/340px-Gal_nations_edward_i.jpg 2x" data-file-width="366" data-file-height="582" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Gal_nations_edward_i.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Edward imposed his authority over church and feudal society, annexed Wales and invaded Scotland</div></div></div>\n<p>Because of his legal reforms Edward is sometimes called <i>The English <a href="/wiki/Justinian_I" title="Justinian I">Justinian</a></i>,<sup id="cite_ref-caterhistory_18-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-caterhistory-18">&#91;18&#93;</a></sup> although whether he was a reformer or an autocrat responding to events is debated. His campaigns left him in debt. This necessitated that he gain wider national support for his policies among lesser landowners, merchants and traders so that he could raise taxes through frequently summoned Parliaments. When <a href="/wiki/Philip_IV_of_France" title="Philip IV of France">Philip IV of France</a> confiscated the <a href="/wiki/Gascony" title="Gascony">duchy of Gascony</a> in 1294, more money was needed to wage war in France. To gain financial support for the war effort, Edward summoned a precedent-setting assembly known as the Model Parliament, which included barons, clergy, knights and townspeople.<sup id="cite_ref-caterhistory_18-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-caterhistory-18">&#91;18&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Edward imposed his authority on the Church with the <a href="/wiki/Statutes_of_Mortmain" title="Statutes of Mortmain">Statutes of Mortmain</a> that prohibited the donation of land to the Church, asserted the rights of the Crown at the expense of traditional feudal privileges, promoted the uniform administration of justice, raised income and codified the legal system. He also emphasised the role of Parliament and the common law through significant legislation, a <a href="/wiki/Hundred_Rolls" title="Hundred Rolls">survey of local government</a> and the codification of laws originating from Magna Carta with the <a href="/wiki/Statute_of_Westminster_1275" title="Statute of Westminster 1275">Statute of Westminster 1275</a>. Edward also enacted economic reforms on wool exports to take customs, which amounted to nearly \xc2\xa310,000 a year, and imposed licence fees on gifts of land to the Church. Feudal jurisdiction was regulated by the <a href="/wiki/Statute_of_Gloucester" title="Statute of Gloucester">Statute of Gloucester</a> and <a href="/wiki/Quo_Warranto" class="mw-redirect" title="Quo Warranto">Quo Warranto</a>. The <a href="/wiki/Statute_of_Winchester" title="Statute of Winchester">Statute of Winchester</a> enforced Plantagenet policing authority. The <a href="/wiki/Statute_of_Westminster_1285" title="Statute of Westminster 1285">Statute of Westminster 1285</a> kept estates within families: tenants only held property for life and were unable to sell the property. <a href="/wiki/Quia_Emptores" title="Quia Emptores">Quia Emptores</a> stopped sub-infeudation where tenants subcontracted their properties and related feudal services.<sup id="cite_ref-PollackandMaitland_19-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-PollackandMaitland-19">&#91;19&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h6><span class="mw-headline" id="Expulsion_of_the_Jews">Expulsion of the Jews</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=England_in_the_Late_Middle_Ages&amp;action=edit&amp;section=9" title="Edit section: Expulsion of the Jews">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h6>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/History_of_the_Jews_in_England_(1066%E2%80%931290)#Increasing_persecution,_13th_century" title="History of the Jews in England (1066\xe2\x80\x931290)">History of the Jews in England (1066\xe2\x80\x931290) \xc2\xa7&#160;Increasing persecution, 13th century</a>; and <a href="/wiki/Edict_of_Expulsion" title="Edict of Expulsion">Edict of Expulsion</a></div>\n<p>The oppression of Jews following their exclusion from the guarantees of Magna Carta peaked with Edward expelling them from England.<sup id="cite_ref-Rubenstein_1996_36_20-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Rubenstein_1996_36-20">&#91;20&#93;</a></sup> Christians were forbidden by <a href="/wiki/Canon_law" title="Canon law">canon law</a> from providing <a href="/wiki/Loans_and_interest_in_Judaism" title="Loans and interest in Judaism">loans with interest</a>, so the Jews played a key economic role in the country by providing this service. In turn the Plantagenets took advantage of the Jews\' status as direct subjects, heavily taxing them at will without the necessity to summon Parliament.<sup id="cite_ref-Rubenstein_1996_37_21-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Rubenstein_1996_37-21">&#91;21&#93;</a></sup> Edward\'s first major step towards Jewish expulsion was the <a href="/wiki/Statute_of_Jewry" title="Statute of Jewry">Statute of Jewry</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-Rubenstein_1996_36_20-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Rubenstein_1996_36-20">&#91;20&#93;</a></sup> which outlawed all <a href="/wiki/Usury" title="Usury">usury</a> and gave Jews fifteen years to buy agricultural land. However, popular prejudice made Jewish movement into mercantile or agricultural pursuits impossible.<sup id="cite_ref-Prestwich1997p345_22-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Prestwich1997p345-22">&#91;22&#93;</a></sup> Edward attempted to clear his debts with the expulsion of Jews from Gascony, seizing their property and transferred all outstanding debts payable to himself.<sup id="cite_ref-Prestwich1997p346_23-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Prestwich1997p346-23">&#91;23&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Prestwich1997p306_24-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Prestwich1997p306-24">&#91;24&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Prestwich1997p307_25-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Prestwich1997p307-25">&#91;25&#93;</a></sup> He made his continued tax demands more palatable to his subjects by offering to expel all Jews in exchange.<sup id="cite_ref-Prestwich1997p343_26-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Prestwich1997p343-26">&#91;26&#93;</a></sup> The heavy tax was passed and the Edict of Expulsion was issued. This proved widely popular and was quickly carried out.<sup id="cite_ref-Mundill2002_27-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Mundill2002-27">&#91;27&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h5><span class="mw-headline" id="Anglo-Scottish_wars">Anglo-Scottish wars</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=England_in_the_Late_Middle_Ages&amp;action=edit&amp;section=10" title="Edit section: Anglo-Scottish wars">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h5>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/First_War_of_Scottish_Independence" title="First War of Scottish Independence">First War of Scottish Independence</a></div>\n<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Battle_from_Holkham_Bible.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1e/Battle_from_Holkham_Bible.jpg/170px-Battle_from_Holkham_Bible.jpg" width="170" height="218" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1e/Battle_from_Holkham_Bible.jpg/255px-Battle_from_Holkham_Bible.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1e/Battle_from_Holkham_Bible.jpg/340px-Battle_from_Holkham_Bible.jpg 2x" data-file-width="799" data-file-height="1024" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Battle_from_Holkham_Bible.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Scene from the Holkham Bible, shows knights and foot soldiers from the period of Bannockburn</div></div></div>\n<p>Edward asserted that the king of Scotland owed him feudal allegiance, which embittered Anglo-Scottish relations for the rest of his reign. Edward intended to create a <a href="/wiki/Dual_monarchy" title="Dual monarchy">dual monarchy</a> by marrying his son <a href="/wiki/Edward_II_of_England" title="Edward II of England">Edward</a> to <a href="/wiki/Margaret,_Maid_of_Norway" title="Margaret, Maid of Norway">Margaret, Maid of Norway</a>, who was the sole heir of <a href="/wiki/Alexander_III_of_Scotland" title="Alexander III of Scotland">Alexander III of Scotland</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-28" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-28">&#91;28&#93;</a></sup> When Margaret died there was no obvious heir to the Scottish throne. Edward was invited by the Scottish magnates to resolve the dispute. Edward obtained recognition from the <a href="/wiki/Competitors_for_the_Crown_of_Scotland" title="Competitors for the Crown of Scotland">competitors for the Scottish throne</a> that he had the \'sovereign lordship of Scotland and the right to determine our several pretensions\'. He decided the case in favour of <a href="/wiki/John_Balliol" title="John Balliol">John Balliol</a>, who duly swore loyalty to him and became king.<sup id="cite_ref-29" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-29">&#91;29&#93;</a></sup> Edward insisted that Scotland was not independent and that as sovereign lord he had the right to hear in England appeals against Balliol\'s judgements, undermining Balliol\'s authority. At the urging of his chief councillors, John entered into an alliance with France in 1295.<sup id="cite_ref-MacDougall2001_30-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-MacDougall2001-30">&#91;30&#93;</a></sup> In 1296 Edward invaded Scotland, deposing and exiling Balliol.<sup id="cite_ref-31" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-31">&#91;31&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Edward was less successful in Gascony, which was overrun by the French. His commitments were beginning to outweigh his resources. Chronic debts had been incurred by wars against Flanders and Gascony in France, and Wales and Scotland in Britain. The clergy refused to pay their share of the costs, with the Archbishop of Canterbury threatening excommunication; Parliament was reluctant to contribute to Edward\'s expensive and unsuccessful military policies. <a href="/wiki/Humphrey_de_Bohun,_3rd_Earl_of_Hereford" title="Humphrey de Bohun, 3rd Earl of Hereford">Humphrey de Bohun, 3rd Earl of Hereford</a> and <a href="/wiki/Roger_Bigod,_5th_Earl_of_Norfolk" title="Roger Bigod, 5th Earl of Norfolk">Roger Bigod, 5th Earl of Norfolk</a>, refused to serve in Gascony, and the barons presented a formal statement of their grievances. Edward was forced to reconfirm the charters (including Magna Carta) to obtain the money he required. A truce and peace treaty with the French king restored the duchy of Gascony to Edward. Meanwhile, <a href="/wiki/William_Wallace" title="William Wallace">William Wallace</a> had risen in Balliol\'s name and recovered most of Scotland, before being defeated at the <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Falkirk_(1298)" class="mw-redirect" title="Battle of Falkirk (1298)">Battle of Falkirk</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-historytoday_32-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-historytoday-32">&#91;32&#93;</a></sup> Edward summoned a full Parliament, including elected Scottish representatives for the settlement of Scotland. The new government in Scotland featured <a href="/wiki/Robert_the_Bruce" title="Robert the Bruce">Robert the Bruce</a>, but he rebelled and was crowned king of Scotland. Despite failing health, Edward was carried north to pursue another campaign, but he died en route at <a href="/wiki/Burgh_by_Sands" title="Burgh by Sands">Burgh by Sands</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-historytoday_32-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-historytoday-32">&#91;32&#93;</a></sup> Even though Edward had requested that his bones should be carried on Scottish campaigns and that his heart be taken to the Holy Land, he was buried at Westminster Abbey in a plain black marble tomb that in later years was painted with the words Scottorum malleus (Hammer of the Scots) and Pactum serva (Honour the vow). He was succeeded by his son as Edward II.<sup id="cite_ref-Jones2012p362_33-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Jones2012p362-33">&#91;33&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h4><span id="Edward_II_.281307.E2.80.9327.29"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Edward_II_(1307\xe2\x80\x9327)">Edward II (1307\xe2\x80\x9327)</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=England_in_the_Late_Middle_Ages&amp;action=edit&amp;section=11" title="Edit section: Edward II (1307\xe2\x80\x9327)">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Edward_II_of_England" title="Edward II of England">Edward II of England</a></div>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Edward_II_-_British_Library_Royal_20_A_ii_f10_(detail).jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d1/Edward_II_-_British_Library_Royal_20_A_ii_f10_%28detail%29.jpg/220px-Edward_II_-_British_Library_Royal_20_A_ii_f10_%28detail%29.jpg" width="220" height="217" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d1/Edward_II_-_British_Library_Royal_20_A_ii_f10_%28detail%29.jpg/330px-Edward_II_-_British_Library_Royal_20_A_ii_f10_%28detail%29.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d1/Edward_II_-_British_Library_Royal_20_A_ii_f10_%28detail%29.jpg/440px-Edward_II_-_British_Library_Royal_20_A_ii_f10_%28detail%29.jpg 2x" data-file-width="957" data-file-height="944" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Edward_II_-_British_Library_Royal_20_A_ii_f10_(detail).jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Edward_II_of_England" title="Edward II of England">Edward II</a> shown receiving the English crown in a contemporary illustration</div></div></div>\n<p>Edward II\'s coronation oath on his succession in 1307 was the first to reflect the king\'s responsibility to maintain the laws that the community "shall have chosen" ("<i>aura eslu</i>").<sup id="cite_ref-McKisack19594_34-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-McKisack19594-34">&#91;34&#93;</a></sup> The king was initially popular but faced three challenges: discontent over the financing of wars; his household spending and the role of his <a href="/wiki/Favourite" title="Favourite">favourite</a> <a href="/wiki/Piers_Gaveston" class="mw-redirect" title="Piers Gaveston">Piers Gaveston</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Maddicott71_35-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Maddicott71-35">&#91;35&#93;</a></sup> When Parliament decided that Gaveston should be exiled the king had no choice but to comply.<sup id="cite_ref-McKisack19596_36-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-McKisack19596-36">&#91;36&#93;</a></sup> The king engineered Gaveston\'s return, but was forced to agree to the appointment of <a href="/wiki/Ordainers" class="mw-redirect" title="Ordainers">Ordainers</a>, led by his cousin <a href="/wiki/Thomas,_2nd_Earl_of_Lancaster" title="Thomas, 2nd Earl of Lancaster">Thomas, 2nd Earl of Lancaster</a>, to reform the royal household with Piers Gaveston exiled again.<sup id="cite_ref-Maddicott103_37-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Maddicott103-37">&#91;37&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-McKisack195910_38-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-McKisack195910-38">&#91;38&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h5><span class="mw-headline" id="Great_Famine">Great Famine</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=England_in_the_Late_Middle_Ages&amp;action=edit&amp;section=12" title="Edit section: Great Famine">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h5>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Great_Famine_of_1315%E2%80%931317" class="mw-redirect" title="Great Famine of 1315\xe2\x80\x931317">Great Famine of 1315\xe2\x80\x931317</a></div>\n<p>In the spring of 1315 unusually heavy rain began in much of Europe. Throughout the spring and summer, it continued to rain and the temperature remained cool. These conditions caused widespread crop failures.<sup id="cite_ref-39" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-39">&#91;39&#93;</a></sup> The straw and hay for the animals could not be cured and there was no fodder for livestock. The price of food began to rise, doubling in England between spring and midsummer.<sup id="cite_ref-40" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-40">&#91;40&#93;</a></sup> Salt, the only way to cure and preserve meat, was difficult to obtain because it could not be extracted through evaporation in the wet weather; it peaked in price in the period 1310\xe2\x80\x9320, reaching double the price from the decade before.<sup id="cite_ref-41" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-41">&#91;41&#93;</a></sup> In the spring of 1316 it continued to rain on a European population deprived of energy and reserves to sustain itself. All segments of society from nobles to peasants were affected, but especially the peasants who were the overwhelming majority of the population and who had no reserve food supplies.<sup id="cite_ref-42" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-42">&#91;42&#93;</a></sup> The height of the famine was reached in 1317 as the wet weather continued. Finally, in the summer the weather returned to its normal patterns. By now, however, people were so weakened by diseases such as <a href="/wiki/Pneumonia" title="Pneumonia">pneumonia</a>, <a href="/wiki/Bronchitis" title="Bronchitis">bronchitis</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Tuberculosis" title="Tuberculosis">tuberculosis</a>, and so much of the seed stock had been eaten, that it was not until 1325 that the food supply returned to relatively normal conditions and the population began to increase again.<sup id="cite_ref-43" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-43">&#91;43&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h5><span class="mw-headline" id="Late_reign_and_deposition">Late reign and deposition</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=England_in_the_Late_Middle_Ages&amp;action=edit&amp;section=13" title="Edit section: Late reign and deposition">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h5>\n<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Retour_d_Isabelle_de_France_en_Angleterre.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/05/Retour_d_Isabelle_de_France_en_Angleterre.jpg/170px-Retour_d_Isabelle_de_France_en_Angleterre.jpg" width="170" height="247" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/05/Retour_d_Isabelle_de_France_en_Angleterre.jpg/255px-Retour_d_Isabelle_de_France_en_Angleterre.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/05/Retour_d_Isabelle_de_France_en_Angleterre.jpg/340px-Retour_d_Isabelle_de_France_en_Angleterre.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1590" data-file-height="2307" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Retour_d_Isabelle_de_France_en_Angleterre.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Isabella_of_France" title="Isabella of France">Queen Isabella</a> landing in England with her son, the future <a href="/wiki/Edward_III_of_England" title="Edward III of England">Edward III</a> in 1326</div></div></div>\n<p>The Ordinances were published widely to obtain maximum popular support but there was a struggle over their repeal or continuation for a decade.<sup id="cite_ref-Maddicott117_44-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Maddicott117-44">&#91;44&#93;</a></sup> When Gaveston returned again to England, he was abducted and executed after a mock trial.<sup id="cite_ref-McKisack195925_45-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-McKisack195925-45">&#91;45&#93;</a></sup> This brutal act drove <a href="/wiki/Thomas,_2nd_Earl_of_Lancaster" title="Thomas, 2nd Earl of Lancaster">Thomas, 2nd Earl of Lancaster</a>, and his adherents from power. Edward\'s humiliating defeat at the <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Bannockburn" title="Battle of Bannockburn">Battle of Bannockburn</a> in 1314 confirmed Bruce\'s position as an independent king of Scots. In England it returned the initiative to Lancaster and <a href="/wiki/Guy_de_Beauchamp,_10th_Earl_of_Warwick" title="Guy de Beauchamp, 10th Earl of Warwick">Guy de Beauchamp, 10th Earl of Warwick</a>, who had not taken part in the campaign, claiming that it was in defiance of the Ordinances.<sup id="cite_ref-Maddicott190_46-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Maddicott190-46">&#91;46&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-McKisack195954_47-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-McKisack195954-47">&#91;47&#93;</a></sup> Edward finally repealed the Ordinances after defeating Lancaster at the <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Boroughbridge" title="Battle of Boroughbridge">Battle of Boroughbridge</a> and then executing him in 1322.<sup id="cite_ref-Maddicott311_48-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Maddicott311-48">&#91;48&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>The <a href="/wiki/War_of_Saint-Sardos" title="War of Saint-Sardos">War of Saint-Sardos</a>, a short conflict between Edward and the <a href="/wiki/France_in_the_Middle_Ages" title="France in the Middle Ages">Kingdom of France</a>, led indirectly to Edward\'s overthrow. The French monarchy used the jurisdiction of the <a href="/wiki/Parlement_of_Paris" class="mw-redirect" title="Parlement of Paris">Parlement of Paris</a> to overrule decisions of the nobility\'s courts. As a French vassal, Edward felt this encroachment in Gascony with the French kings adjudicating disputes between him and his French subjects. Without confrontation he could do little but watch the duchy shrink. <a href="/wiki/Edmund_of_Woodstock,_1st_Earl_of_Kent" title="Edmund of Woodstock, 1st Earl of Kent">Edmund of Woodstock, 1st Earl of Kent</a>, decided to resist one such judgement in <a href="/wiki/Saint-Sardos,_Lot-et-Garonne" title="Saint-Sardos, Lot-et-Garonne">Saint-Sardos</a> with the result that Charles IV declared the duchy forfeit. Charles\'s sister, <a href="/wiki/Isabella_of_France" title="Isabella of France">Queen Isabella</a>, was sent to negotiate and agreed to a treaty that required Edward to pay homage in France to Charles. Edward resigned Aquitaine and Ponthieu to his son, <a href="/wiki/Edward_III_of_England" title="Edward III of England">Prince Edward</a>, who travelled to France to give homage in his stead. With the English heir in her power, Isabella refused to return to England unless Edward II dismissed his favourites and also formed a relationship with <a href="/wiki/Roger_Mortimer,_1st_Earl_of_March" title="Roger Mortimer, 1st Earl of March">Roger Mortimer, 1st Earl of March</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Jones2012p411_49-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Jones2012p411-49">&#91;49&#93;</a></sup> The couple invaded England and, joined by <a href="/wiki/Henry,_3rd_Earl_of_Lancaster" title="Henry, 3rd Earl of Lancaster">Henry, 3rd Earl of Lancaster</a>, captured the king.<sup id="cite_ref-Mortimer_50-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Mortimer-50">&#91;50&#93;</a></sup> Edward II abdicated on the condition that his son would inherit the throne rather than Mortimer. He is generally believed to have been murdered at Berkeley Castle by having a red-hot poker thrust into his bowels.<sup id="cite_ref-Weir2008_51-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Weir2008-51">&#91;51&#93;</a></sup> In 1330 a coup by Edward III ended four years of control by Isabella and Mortimer. Mortimer was executed, but although removed from power, Isabella was treated well, living in luxury for the next 27 years.<sup id="cite_ref-Jones2012p438_52-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Jones2012p438-52">&#91;52&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h4><span id="Edward_III_.281327.E2.80.9377.29"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Edward_III_(1327\xe2\x80\x9377)">Edward III (1327\xe2\x80\x9377)</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=England_in_the_Late_Middle_Ages&amp;action=edit&amp;section=14" title="Edit section: Edward III (1327\xe2\x80\x9377)">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Edward_III_of_England" title="Edward III of England">Edward III of England</a></div>\n<h5><span class="mw-headline" id="Renewal_of_the_Scottish_War">Renewal of the Scottish War</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=England_in_the_Late_Middle_Ages&amp;action=edit&amp;section=15" title="Edit section: Renewal of the Scottish War">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h5>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Second_War_of_Scottish_Independence" title="Second War of Scottish Independence">Second War of Scottish Independence</a></div>\n<p>After the defeat of the English under the Mortimer regime by the Scots at <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Stanhope_Park" title="Battle of Stanhope Park">Battle of Stanhope Park</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Treaty_of_Edinburgh%E2%80%93Northampton" title="Treaty of Edinburgh\xe2\x80\x93Northampton">Treaty of Edinburgh\xe2\x80\x93Northampton</a> was signed in Edward III\'s name in 1328. Edward was not content with the peace agreement, but the renewal of the war with Scotland originated in private, rather than royal initiative. A group of English <a href="/wiki/Magnate" title="Magnate">magnates</a> known as The Disinherited, who had lost land in Scotland by the peace accord, staged an invasion of Scotland and won a victory at the <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Dupplin_Moor" title="Battle of Dupplin Moor">Battle of Dupplin Moor</a> in 1332.<sup id="cite_ref-53" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-53">&#91;53&#93;</a></sup> They attempted to install <a href="/wiki/Edward_Balliol" title="Edward Balliol">Edward Balliol</a> as king of Scotland in <a href="/wiki/David_II_of_Scotland" title="David II of Scotland">David&#160;II</a>\'s place, but Balliol was soon expelled and was forced to seek the help of Edward&#160;III. The English king responded by laying siege to the important border town of <a href="/wiki/Berwick-upon-Tweed" title="Berwick-upon-Tweed">Berwick</a> and defeated a large relieving army at the <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Halidon_Hill" title="Battle of Halidon Hill">Battle of Halidon Hill</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-54" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-54">&#91;54&#93;</a></sup> Edward reinstated Balliol on the throne and received a substantial amount of land in southern Scotland.<sup id="cite_ref-55" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-55">&#91;55&#93;</a></sup> These victories proved hard to sustain, however, as forces loyal to David&#160;II gradually regained control of the country. In 1338, Edward was forced to agree to a truce with the Scots.<sup id="cite_ref-56" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-56">&#91;56&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h5><span id="Hundred_Years.27_War"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Hundred_Years\'_War">Hundred Years\' War</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=England_in_the_Late_Middle_Ages&amp;action=edit&amp;section=16" title="Edit section: Hundred Years&#039; War">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h5>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Hundred_Years%27_War_(1337%E2%80%931360)" title="Hundred Years&#39; War (1337\xe2\x80\x931360)">Hundred Years\' War (1337\xe2\x80\x931360)</a></div>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Battle_of_crecy_froissart.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/24/Battle_of_crecy_froissart.jpg/220px-Battle_of_crecy_froissart.jpg" width="220" height="193" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/24/Battle_of_crecy_froissart.jpg/330px-Battle_of_crecy_froissart.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/24/Battle_of_crecy_froissart.jpg/440px-Battle_of_crecy_froissart.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1998" data-file-height="1755" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Battle_of_crecy_froissart.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The victory at <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Cr%C3%A9cy" title="Battle of Cr\xc3\xa9cy">Cr\xc3\xa9cy</a> was an important success for the English crown in the <a href="/wiki/Hundred_Years%27_War_(1337%E2%80%931360)" title="Hundred Years&#39; War (1337\xe2\x80\x931360)">Edwardian War</a> in France.</div></div></div>\n<p>In 1328 <a href="/wiki/Charles_IV_of_France" title="Charles IV of France">Charles IV of France</a> died without a male heir. His cousin <a href="/wiki/Philip_VI_of_France" title="Philip VI of France">Phillip of Valois</a> and <a href="/wiki/Isabella_of_France" title="Isabella of France">Queen Isabella</a>, on behalf of her son Edward, were the major claimants to the throne. Philip, as senior grandson of <a href="/wiki/Philip_III_of_France" title="Philip III of France">Philip III of France</a> in the male line, became king over Edward\'s claim as a <a href="/wiki/Matrilineal" class="mw-redirect" title="Matrilineal">matrilineal</a> grandson of Philip IV of France, following the precedents of Philip V\'s succession over his niece <a href="/wiki/Joan_II_of_Navarre" title="Joan II of Navarre">Joan II of Navarre</a> and Charles IV\'s succession over his nieces. Not yet in power, Edward III paid homage to Phillip as Duke of Aquitaine and the French king continued to assert feudal pressure on Gascony.<sup id="cite_ref-Prestwich2005p304_57-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Prestwich2005p304-57">&#91;57&#93;</a></sup> Philip demanded that Edward extradite an exiled French adviser, <a href="/wiki/Robert_III_of_Artois" title="Robert III of Artois">Robert III of Artois</a>, and, when he refused, declared Edward\'s lands in Gascony and <a href="/wiki/Ponthieu" title="Ponthieu">Ponthieu</a> forfeit.<sup id="cite_ref-Prestwich2005p306_58-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Prestwich2005p306-58">&#91;58&#93;</a></sup> In response Edward put together a coalition of continental supporters, promising payment of over \xc2\xa3200,000.<sup id="cite_ref-Prestwich2005p310_59-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Prestwich2005p310-59">&#91;59&#93;</a></sup> Edward borrowed heavily from the banking houses of the <a href="/wiki/Bardi_family" title="Bardi family">Bardi</a> and <a href="/wiki/Peruzzi" title="Peruzzi">Peruzzi</a>, merchants in the Low Countries, and <a href="/wiki/William_de_la_Pole_(Chief_Baron_of_the_Exchequer)" title="William de la Pole (Chief Baron of the Exchequer)">William de la Pole</a>, a wealthy merchant who came to the king\'s rescue by advancing him \xc2\xa3110,000.<sup id="cite_ref-Prestwich2005p273_60-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Prestwich2005p273-60">&#91;60&#93;</a></sup> Edward also asked Parliament for a grant of \xc2\xa3300,000 in return for further concessions.<sup id="cite_ref-Ackroyd2011_61-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Ackroyd2011-61">&#91;61&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>The delay caused by fundraising allowed the French to invade Gascony, and threaten the English ports<sup id="cite_ref-Prestwich2005p311_62-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Prestwich2005p311-62">&#91;62&#93;</a></sup> while the English conducted widespread <a href="/wiki/English_Channel_naval_campaign_(1338%E2%80%931339)" class="mw-redirect" title="English Channel naval campaign (1338\xe2\x80\x931339)">piracy in the Channel</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Sumption_63-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Sumption-63">&#91;63&#93;</a></sup> Edward proclaimed himself king of France to encourage the Flemish to rise in open rebellion against the French king and won a significant naval victory at the <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Sluys" title="Battle of Sluys">Battle of Sluys</a>, where the French fleet was almost completely destroyed. Inconclusive fighting continued at the <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Saint-Omer" title="Battle of Saint-Omer">Battle of Saint-Omer</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Siege_of_Tournai_(1340)" title="Siege of Tournai (1340)">Siege of Tournai (1340)</a>, but with both sides running out of money, the fighting ended with the <a href="/wiki/Truce_of_Espl%C3%A9chin" title="Truce of Espl\xc3\xa9chin">Truce of Espl\xc3\xa9chin</a>. Edward III had achieved nothing of military value and English political opinion was against him. Bankrupt, he cut his losses, ruining many whom he could not, or chose not, to repay.<sup id="cite_ref-Jones2012p471_64-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Jones2012p471-64">&#91;64&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Both countries suffered from war exhaustion. The tax burden had been heavy and the wool trade had been disrupted. Edward spent the following years paying off his immense debt, while the Gascons merged the war with banditry. In 1346 Edward invaded from the Low Countries using the strategy of <a href="/wiki/Chevauch%C3%A9e" title="Chevauch\xc3\xa9e">chevauch\xc3\xa9e</a>, a large extended raid for plunder and destruction that would be deployed by the English throughout the war. The chevauch\xc3\xa9e discredited Philip VI of France\'s government and threatened to detach his vassals from loyalty. Edward fought two successful actions, the <a href="/wiki/Storming_of_Caen" class="mw-redirect" title="Storming of Caen">Storming of Caen</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Blanchetaque" title="Battle of Blanchetaque">Battle of Blanchetaque</a>. He then found himself outmanoeuvred and outnumbered by Philip and was forced to fight at <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Cr%C3%A9cy" title="Battle of Cr\xc3\xa9cy">Cr\xc3\xa9cy</a>. The battle was a crushing defeat for the French, leaving Edward free to capture the important port of <a href="/wiki/Calais" title="Calais">Calais</a>. A subsequent victory against Scotland at the <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Neville%27s_Cross" title="Battle of Neville&#39;s Cross">Battle of Neville\'s Cross</a> resulted in the capture of <a href="/wiki/David_II_of_Scotland" title="David II of Scotland">David II</a> and reduced the threat from Scotland.<sup id="cite_ref-Jones2012p476_65-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Jones2012p476-65">&#91;65&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h5><span class="mw-headline" id="Black_Death">Black Death</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=England_in_the_Late_Middle_Ages&amp;action=edit&amp;section=17" title="Edit section: Black Death">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h5>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Black_Death_in_England" title="Black Death in England">Black Death in England</a></div>\n<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:PlaguePlaque.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/df/PlaguePlaque.jpg/220px-PlaguePlaque.jpg" width="220" height="131" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/df/PlaguePlaque.jpg/330px-PlaguePlaque.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/df/PlaguePlaque.jpg/440px-PlaguePlaque.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1600" data-file-height="952" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:PlaguePlaque.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Plaque in Weymouth, noting the entrance of plague into the country</div></div></div><p>\nAccording to the chronicle of the <a href="/wiki/Franciscan" class="mw-redirect" title="Franciscan">grey friars</a> at <a href="/wiki/King%27s_Lynn" title="King&#39;s Lynn">King\'s Lynn</a>, the plague arrived by ship from <a href="/wiki/Gascony" title="Gascony">Gascony</a> to <a href="/wiki/Melcombe_Regis" title="Melcombe Regis">Melcombe</a> in Dorset&#160;&#8211;&#32;today normally referred to as <a href="/wiki/Weymouth,_Dorset" title="Weymouth, Dorset">Weymouth</a>&#160;&#8211;&#32;shortly before "the <a href="/wiki/Feast_of_St._John_the_Baptist" class="mw-redirect" title="Feast of St. John the Baptist">Feast of St. John The Baptist</a>" on 24 June 1348.<sup id="cite_ref-66" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-66">&#91;66&#93;</a></sup> From Weymouth the disease spread rapidly across the south-west. The first major city to be struck was <a href="/wiki/Bristol" title="Bristol">Bristol</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-67" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-67">&#91;67&#93;</a></sup> London was reached in the autumn of 1348, before most of the surrounding countryside. This had certainly happened by November, though according to some accounts as early as 29 September.<sup id="cite_ref-68" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-68">&#91;68&#93;</a></sup> The full effect of the plague was felt in the capital early the next year.<sup id="cite_ref-69" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-69">&#91;69&#93;</a></sup> Conditions in London were ideal for the plague: the streets were narrow and flowing with sewage, and houses were overcrowded and poorly ventilated.<sup id="cite_ref-70" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-70">&#91;70&#93;</a></sup> By March 1349 the disease was spreading in a haphazard way across all of southern England.<sup id="cite_ref-71" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-71">&#91;71&#93;</a></sup> During the first half of 1349 the Black Death spread northwards. A second front opened up when the plague arrived by ship at the <a href="/wiki/Humber" title="Humber">Humber</a>, from where it spread both south and north.<sup id="cite_ref-72" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-72">&#91;72&#93;</a></sup> In May it reached <a href="/wiki/York" title="York">York</a>, and during the summer months of June, July and August, it ravaged the north.<sup id="cite_ref-73" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-73">&#91;73&#93;</a></sup> Certain northern counties, like <a href="/wiki/County_Durham" title="County Durham">Durham</a> and <a href="/wiki/Cumberland" title="Cumberland">Cumberland</a>, had been the victim of violent incursions from the Scots, and were therefore left particularly vulnerable to the devastations of the plague.<sup id="cite_ref-74" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-74">&#91;74&#93;</a></sup> Pestilence is less virulent during the winter months, and spreads less rapidly.<sup id="cite_ref-75" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-75">&#91;75&#93;</a></sup> The Black Death in England had survived the winter of 1348\xe2\x80\x9349, but during the following winter it ended, and by December 1349 conditions were returning to relative normalcy.<sup id="cite_ref-76" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-76">&#91;76&#93;</a></sup> It had taken the disease approximately 500 days to traverse the entire country.<sup id="cite_ref-77" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-77">&#91;77&#93;</a></sup> The Black Death brought a halt to Edward\'s campaigns by killing between a third to more than half of his subjects.<sup id="cite_ref-Prestwich2005p531_78-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Prestwich2005p531-78">&#91;78&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-79" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-79">&#91;79&#93;</a></sup> The king passed the <a href="/wiki/Ordinance_of_Labourers" class="mw-redirect" title="Ordinance of Labourers">Ordinance of Labourers</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Statute_of_Labourers_1351" title="Statute of Labourers 1351">Statute of Labourers</a> in response to the shortage of labour and social unrest that followed the plague.<sup id="cite_ref-Prestwich2005p548_80-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Prestwich2005p548-80">&#91;80&#93;</a></sup> The labour laws were ineffectively enforced and the repressive measures caused resentment.<sup id="cite_ref-Jones2012_81-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Jones2012-81">&#91;81&#93;</a></sup></p><div style="clear:both;"></div>\n<h5><span id="Poitiers_campaign_and_expansion_of_the_conflict_.281356.E2.80.9368.29"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Poitiers_campaign_and_expansion_of_the_conflict_(1356\xe2\x80\x9368)">Poitiers campaign and expansion of the conflict (1356\xe2\x80\x9368)</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=England_in_the_Late_Middle_Ages&amp;action=edit&amp;section=18" title="Edit section: Poitiers campaign and expansion of the conflict (1356\xe2\x80\x9368)">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h5>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Edward_III_Black_Prince_14thc.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9e/Edward_III_Black_Prince_14thc.jpg/220px-Edward_III_Black_Prince_14thc.jpg" width="220" height="254" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9e/Edward_III_Black_Prince_14thc.jpg/330px-Edward_III_Black_Prince_14thc.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9e/Edward_III_Black_Prince_14thc.jpg/440px-Edward_III_Black_Prince_14thc.jpg 2x" data-file-width="771" data-file-height="889" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Edward_III_Black_Prince_14thc.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Edward,_the_Black_Prince" class="mw-redirect" title="Edward, the Black Prince">Edward, the Black Prince</a> is granted Aquitaine by his father King Edward III. Initial letter "E" of miniature, 1390; British Library, shelfmark: <a href="/wiki/Cotton_MS_Nero" class="mw-redirect" title="Cotton MS Nero">Cotton MS Nero</a> D VI, f.31</div></div></div>\n<p>In 1356 <a href="/wiki/Edward,_the_Black_Prince" class="mw-redirect" title="Edward, the Black Prince">Edward, Prince of Wales</a>, resumed the war with one of the most destructive chevauch\xc3\xa9es of the conflict. Starting from Bordeaux he laid waste to the lands of Armagnac before turning eastward into Languedoc. Toulouse prepared for a siege, but the Prince\'s army was not equipped for one, so he bypassed the city and continued south, pillaging and burning. Unlike large cities such as Toulouse, the rural French villages were not organised to provide a defence, making them much more attractive targets. In a second great chevauch\xc3\xa9e the Prince burned the suburbs of Bourges without capturing the city, before marching west along the Loire River to Poitiers where the <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Poitiers" title="Battle of Poitiers">Battle of Poitiers</a> resulted in a decisive English victory and the capture of <a href="/wiki/John_II_of_France" title="John II of France">John II of France</a>. The <a href="/wiki/Second_Treaty_of_London" class="mw-redirect" title="Second Treaty of London">Second Treaty of London</a> was signed, which promised a four million \xc3\xa9cus ransom. It was guaranteed by the Valois family hostages being held in London, while John returned to France to raise his ransom. Edward gained possession of Normandy, Brittany, Anjou, Maine and the coastline from Flanders to Spain, restoring the lands of the former Angevin Empire. The hostages quickly escaped back to France. John, horrified that his word had been broken, returned to England and died there. Edward invaded France in an attempt to take advantage of the popular rebellion of the <a href="/wiki/Jacquerie" title="Jacquerie">Jacquerie</a>, hoping to seize the throne. Although no French army stood against him, he was unable to take Paris or <a href="/wiki/Rheims" class="mw-redirect" title="Rheims">Rheims</a>. In the subsequent <a href="/wiki/Treaty_of_Br%C3%A9tigny" title="Treaty of Br\xc3\xa9tigny">Treaty of Br\xc3\xa9tigny</a> he renounced his claim to the French crown, but greatly expanded his territory in Aquitaine and confirmed his conquest of Calais.<sup id="cite_ref-82" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-82">&#91;82&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Fighting in the Hundred Years\' War often spilled from the French and Plantagenet lands into surrounding realms. This included the dynastic conflict in Castile between <a href="/wiki/Peter_of_Castile" title="Peter of Castile">Peter of Castile</a> and <a href="/wiki/Henry_II_of_Castile" title="Henry II of Castile">Henry II of Castile</a>. Edward, Prince of Wales, allied himself with Peter, defeating Henry at the <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_N%C3%A1jera" title="Battle of N\xc3\xa1jera">Battle of N\xc3\xa1jera</a> before falling out with Peter, who had no means to reimburse him, leaving Edward bankrupt. The Plantagenets continued to interfere and <a href="/wiki/John_of_Gaunt,_1st_Duke_of_Lancaster" class="mw-redirect" title="John of Gaunt, 1st Duke of Lancaster">John of Gaunt, 1st Duke of Lancaster</a>, the prince\'s brother, married Peter\'s daughter <a href="/wiki/Infanta_Constance_of_Castile" class="mw-redirect" title="Infanta Constance of Castile">Constance</a>, claiming the Crown of Castile in the name of his wife. He arrived with an army, asking <a href="/wiki/John_I_of_Castile" title="John I of Castile">John I</a> to give up the throne in favour of Constance. John declined; instead his son married John of Gaunt\'s daughter, <a href="/wiki/Catherine_of_Lancaster" title="Catherine of Lancaster">Catherine of Lancaster</a>, creating the title <a href="/wiki/Prince_of_Asturias" title="Prince of Asturias">Prince of Asturias</a> for the couple.<sup id="cite_ref-Weir102_83-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Weir102-83">&#91;83&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h5><span id="French_resurgence_.281369.E2.80.9377.29"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="French_resurgence_(1369\xe2\x80\x9377)">French resurgence (1369\xe2\x80\x9377)</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=England_in_the_Late_Middle_Ages&amp;action=edit&amp;section=19" title="Edit section: French resurgence (1369\xe2\x80\x9377)">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h5>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Hundred_Years%27_War_(1369%E2%80%931389)" class="mw-redirect" title="Hundred Years&#39; War (1369\xe2\x80\x931389)">Hundred Years\' War (1369\xe2\x80\x931389)</a></div>\n<p><a href="/wiki/Charles_V_of_France" title="Charles V of France">Charles V of France</a> resumed hostilities when Prince Edward refused a summons as <a href="/wiki/Duke_of_Aquitaine" title="Duke of Aquitaine">Duke of Aquitaine</a> and his reign saw the Plantagenets steadily pushed back in France.<sup id="cite_ref-wagner122_84-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-wagner122-84">&#91;84&#93;</a></sup> Prince Edward went on to demonstrate the brutal character that some think is the cause of his name of the "Black Prince" at the <a href="/wiki/Siege_of_Limoges" title="Siege of Limoges">Siege of Limoges</a>. After the town had opened its gates to <a href="/wiki/John,_Duke_of_Berry" title="John, Duke of Berry">John, Duke of Berry</a>, he directed the massacre of 3,000 inhabitants, men, women and children.<sup id="cite_ref-Jones2012p518_85-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Jones2012p518-85">&#91;85&#93;</a></sup> Following this the prince was too ill to contribute to the war or government and returned to England where he soon died: the son of a king and the father of a king, but never a king himself.<sup id="cite_ref-Weir2008p93_86-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Weir2008p93-86">&#91;86&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Prince Edward\'s brother <a href="/wiki/John_of_Gaunt,_1st_Duke_of_Lancaster" class="mw-redirect" title="John of Gaunt, 1st Duke of Lancaster">John of Gaunt, 1st Duke of Lancaster</a> assumed leadership of the English in France. Despite further chevauch\xc3\xa9es, destroying the countryside and the productivity of the land, his efforts were strategically ineffective.<sup id="cite_ref-Sumption325_87-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Sumption325-87">&#91;87&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Sumption187_88-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Sumption187-88">&#91;88&#93;</a></sup> The French commander, <a href="/wiki/Bertrand_Du_Guesclin" class="mw-redirect" title="Bertrand Du Guesclin">Bertrand Du Guesclin</a> adopted <a href="/wiki/Fabian_Strategy" class="mw-redirect" title="Fabian Strategy">Fabian tactics</a> in avoiding major English field forces while capturing towns, including <a href="/wiki/Poitiers" title="Poitiers">Poitiers</a> and <a href="/wiki/Bergerac,_Dordogne" title="Bergerac, Dordogne">Bergerac</a>. In a further strategic blow, English dominance at sea was reversed by the disastrous defeat at the <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_La_Rochelle" title="Battle of La Rochelle">Battle of La Rochelle</a>, undermining English seaborne trade and allowing Gascony to be threatened.<sup id="cite_ref-SherborneTuck1994_89-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-SherborneTuck1994-89">&#91;89&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h4><span id="Richard_II_.281377.E2.80.9399.29"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Richard_II_(1377\xe2\x80\x9399)">Richard II (1377\xe2\x80\x9399)</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=England_in_the_Late_Middle_Ages&amp;action=edit&amp;section=20" title="Edit section: Richard II (1377\xe2\x80\x9399)">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Richard_II_of_England" title="Richard II of England">Richard II of England</a></div>\n<h5><span id="Peasants.27_Revolt"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Peasants\'_Revolt">Peasants\' Revolt</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=England_in_the_Late_Middle_Ages&amp;action=edit&amp;section=21" title="Edit section: Peasants&#039;
