Revolt">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h5>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Peasant%27s_Revolt" class="mw-redirect" title="Peasant&#39;s Revolt">Peasant\'s Revolt</a></div>\n<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Jean_Froissart,_Chroniques,_154v,_12148_btv1b8438605hf336,_crop.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/33/Jean_Froissart%2C_Chroniques%2C_154v%2C_12148_btv1b8438605hf336%2C_crop.jpg/220px-Jean_Froissart%2C_Chroniques%2C_154v%2C_12148_btv1b8438605hf336%2C_crop.jpg" width="220" height="212" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/33/Jean_Froissart%2C_Chroniques%2C_154v%2C_12148_btv1b8438605hf336%2C_crop.jpg/330px-Jean_Froissart%2C_Chroniques%2C_154v%2C_12148_btv1b8438605hf336%2C_crop.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/33/Jean_Froissart%2C_Chroniques%2C_154v%2C_12148_btv1b8438605hf336%2C_crop.jpg/440px-Jean_Froissart%2C_Chroniques%2C_154v%2C_12148_btv1b8438605hf336%2C_crop.jpg 2x" data-file-width="3261" data-file-height="3144" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Jean_Froissart,_Chroniques,_154v,_12148_btv1b8438605hf336,_crop.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Richard_II_of_England" title="Richard II of England">Richard II</a> meets the rebels of the <a href="/wiki/Peasants%27_Revolt" title="Peasants&#39; Revolt">Peasants\' Revolt</a> in a painting from Froissart\'s <i>Chronicles</i>.</div></div></div>\n<p>The 10-year-old Richard II succeeded on the deaths of his father and grandfather, with government in the hands of a regency council until he came of age.<sup id="cite_ref-90" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-90">&#91;90&#93;</a></sup> The poor state of the economy caused significant civil unrest as his government levied a number of <a href="/wiki/Taxation_in_medieval_England" title="Taxation in medieval England">poll taxes</a> to finance military campaigns.<sup id="cite_ref-91" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-91">&#91;91&#93;</a></sup> The tax of one shilling for everyone over the age of 15 proved particularly unpopular. This, combined with enforcement of the <a href="/wiki/Statute_of_Labourers" class="mw-redirect" title="Statute of Labourers">Statute of Labourers</a>, which curbed employment standards and wages,<sup id="cite_ref-Jones2012p493_92-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Jones2012p493-92">&#91;92&#93;</a></sup> triggered an uprising with a refusal to pay the tax in 1381. Kent rebels, led by <a href="/wiki/Wat_Tyler" title="Wat Tyler">Wat Tyler</a>, marched on London. Initially, there were only attacks on certain properties, many of them associated with John of Gaunt. The rebels are reputed to have been met by the young king himself and presented him with a series of demands, including the dismissal of some of his ministers and the abolition of <a href="/wiki/Serfdom" title="Serfdom">serfdom</a>. Rebels stormed the <a href="/wiki/Tower_of_London" title="Tower of London">Tower of London</a> and executed those hiding there.<sup id="cite_ref-Jones2012p532_93-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Jones2012p532-93">&#91;93&#93;</a></sup> At <a href="/wiki/Smithfield,_London" title="Smithfield, London">Smithfield</a> further negotiations were arranged, but Tyler behaved belligerently and in the ensuing dispute <a href="/wiki/William_Walworth" title="William Walworth">William Walworth</a>, the Lord Mayor of London, attacked and killed Tyler. Richard seized the initiative shouting "You shall have no captain but me",<sup id="cite_ref-Schama2000_94-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Schama2000-94">&#91;94&#93;</a></sup> a statement left deliberately ambiguous to defuse the situation.<sup id="cite_ref-95" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-95">&#91;95&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-96" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-96">&#91;96&#93;</a></sup> He had promised clemency, but on re-establishing control he pursued, captured and executed the other leaders of the rebellion and all concessions were revoked.<sup id="cite_ref-Jones2012p540_97-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Jones2012p540-97">&#91;97&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h5><span class="mw-headline" id="Deposition">Deposition</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=England_in_the_Late_Middle_Ages&amp;action=edit&amp;section=22" title="Edit section: Deposition">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h5>\n<p>A group of <a href="/wiki/Magnates" class="mw-redirect" title="Magnates">magnates</a> consisting of the king\'s uncle <a href="/wiki/Thomas_of_Woodstock,_1st_Duke_of_Gloucester" title="Thomas of Woodstock, 1st Duke of Gloucester">Thomas of Woodstock, 1st Duke of Gloucester</a>, <a href="/wiki/Richard_FitzAlan,_11th_Earl_of_Arundel" title="Richard FitzAlan, 11th Earl of Arundel">Richard FitzAlan, 11th Earl of Arundel</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Thomas_de_Beauchamp,_12th_Earl_of_Warwick" title="Thomas de Beauchamp, 12th Earl of Warwick">Thomas de Beauchamp, 12th Earl of Warwick</a>, became known as the <a href="/wiki/Lords_Appellant" title="Lords Appellant">Lords Appellant</a> when they sought to impeach five of the king\'s favourites and restrain what was increasingly seen as tyrannical and capricious rule.<sup id="cite_ref-richtwo_98-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-richtwo-98">&#91;98&#93;</a></sup> Later they were joined by <a href="/wiki/Henry_IV_of_England" title="Henry IV of England">Henry Bolingbroke</a>, the son and heir of John of Gaunt, and <a href="/wiki/Thomas_de_Mowbray,_1st_Duke_of_Norfolk" title="Thomas de Mowbray, 1st Duke of Norfolk">Thomas de Mowbray, 1st Duke of Norfolk</a>. Initially, they were successful in establishing a commission to govern England for one year, but they were forced to rebel against Richard, defeating an army under <a href="/wiki/Robert_de_Vere,_Duke_of_Ireland" title="Robert de Vere, Duke of Ireland">Robert de Vere, Earl of Oxford</a>, at the skirmish of <a href="/wiki/Radcot_Bridge_(Battle)" class="mw-redirect" title="Radcot Bridge (Battle)">Radcot Bridge</a>. Richard was reduced to a figurehead with little power. As a result of the <a href="/wiki/Merciless_Parliament" title="Merciless Parliament">Merciless Parliament</a>, de Vere and <a href="/wiki/Michael_de_la_Pole,_1st_Earl_of_Suffolk" title="Michael de la Pole, 1st Earl of Suffolk">Michael de la Pole, 1st Earl of Suffolk</a>, who had fled abroad, were sentenced to death in their absence. <a href="/wiki/Alexander_Neville" title="Alexander Neville">Alexander Neville, Archbishop of York</a>, had all of his worldly goods confiscated. A number of Richard\'s council were executed. Following John of Gaunt\'s return from Spain, Richard was able to rebuild his power, having Gloucester murdered in captivity in Calais. Warwick was stripped of his title. Bolingbroke and Mowbray were exiled.<sup id="cite_ref-richtwo_98-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-richtwo-98">&#91;98&#93;</a></sup> When John of Gaunt died in 1399, Richard disinherited Henry of Bolingbroke, who invaded England in response with a small force that quickly grew in numbers. Meeting little resistance, Henry deposed Richard to have himself crowned Henry IV of England. Richard died in captivity early the next year, probably murdered, bringing an end to the main Plantagenet line.<sup id="cite_ref-Jones2012p601_99-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Jones2012p601-99">&#91;99&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="House_of_Lancaster">House of Lancaster</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=England_in_the_Late_Middle_Ages&amp;action=edit&amp;section=23" title="Edit section: House of Lancaster">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/House_of_Lancaster" title="House of Lancaster">House of Lancaster</a></div>\n<h4><span id="Henry_IV_.281399.E2.80.931413.29"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Henry_IV_(1399\xe2\x80\x931413)">Henry IV (1399\xe2\x80\x931413)</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=England_in_the_Late_Middle_Ages&amp;action=edit&amp;section=24" title="Edit section: Henry IV (1399\xe2\x80\x931413)">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Henry_IV_of_England" title="Henry IV of England">Henry IV of England</a></div>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Battle_of_Agincourt,_St._Alban%27s_Chronicle_by_Thomas_Walsingham.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b9/Battle_of_Agincourt%2C_St._Alban%27s_Chronicle_by_Thomas_Walsingham.jpg/220px-Battle_of_Agincourt%2C_St._Alban%27s_Chronicle_by_Thomas_Walsingham.jpg" width="220" height="183" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b9/Battle_of_Agincourt%2C_St._Alban%27s_Chronicle_by_Thomas_Walsingham.jpg/330px-Battle_of_Agincourt%2C_St._Alban%27s_Chronicle_by_Thomas_Walsingham.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b9/Battle_of_Agincourt%2C_St._Alban%27s_Chronicle_by_Thomas_Walsingham.jpg/440px-Battle_of_Agincourt%2C_St._Alban%27s_Chronicle_by_Thomas_Walsingham.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1500" data-file-height="1249" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Battle_of_Agincourt,_St._Alban%27s_Chronicle_by_Thomas_Walsingham.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Agincourt" title="Battle of Agincourt">Battle of Agincourt</a> fought on <a href="/wiki/Saint_Crispin" class="mw-redirect" title="Saint Crispin">Saint Crispin\'s Day</a> 1415</div></div></div>\n<p>Henry\'s claim to the throne was that his mother had legitimate rights through descent from <a href="/wiki/Edmund_Crouchback" title="Edmund Crouchback">Edmund Crouchback</a>, whom he claimed was the elder son of Henry III of England, set aside due to deformity, but these claims were not widely believed.<sup id="cite_ref-Weir1995_100-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Weir1995-100">&#91;100&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Edmund_Mortimer,_5th_Earl_of_March" title="Edmund Mortimer, 5th Earl of March">Edmund Mortimer, Earl of March</a>, was the heir presumptive to Richard II by being the grandson of <a href="/wiki/Lionel_of_Antwerp,_1st_Duke_of_Clarence" title="Lionel of Antwerp, 1st Duke of Clarence">Lionel of Antwerp, 1st Duke of Clarence</a>. As a child he was not considered a serious contender. He never showed interest in the throne as an adult, instead serving the House of Lancaster loyally. When <a href="/wiki/Richard_of_Conisburgh,_3rd_Earl_of_Cambridge" title="Richard of Conisburgh, 3rd Earl of Cambridge">Richard of Conisburgh, 3rd Earl of Cambridge</a>, later <a href="/wiki/Southampton_Plot" title="Southampton Plot">plotted</a> to use him to displace Henry\'s newly crowned son, and their mutual cousin, Edmund informed the new king and the plotters were executed. However, the later marriage of his <a href="/wiki/Anne_de_Mortimer" title="Anne de Mortimer">granddaughter</a> to Richard\'s <a href="/wiki/Richard_Plantagenet,_3rd_Duke_of_York" class="mw-redirect" title="Richard Plantagenet, 3rd Duke of York">son</a> consolidated his descendants\' claim to the throne with that of the more junior <a href="/wiki/House_of_York" title="House of York">House of York</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Weir1995_100-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Weir1995-100">&#91;100&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Henry resumed war with France, but was plagued with financial problems, declining health and frequent rebellions.<sup id="cite_ref-mortimer353_101-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-mortimer353-101">&#91;101&#93;</a></sup> A Scottish invasion was defeated at the <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Homildon_Hill" title="Battle of Homildon Hill">Battle of Homildon Hill</a>, but it resulted in a long war with <a href="/wiki/Henry_Percy,_1st_Earl_of_Northumberland" title="Henry Percy, 1st Earl of Northumberland">Henry Percy, 1st Earl of Northumberland</a>, for northern England, which was resolved only with the near complete destruction of the <a href="/wiki/House_of_Percy" title="House of Percy">Percy family</a> at the <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Bramham_Moor" title="Battle of Bramham Moor">Battle of Bramham Moor</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-mortimer253_102-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-mortimer253-102">&#91;102&#93;</a></sup> In Wales <a href="/wiki/Owain_Glynd%C5%B5r" title="Owain Glynd\xc5\xb5r">Owain Glynd\xc5\xb5r</a>\'s widespread rebellion was only put down in 1408.<sup id="cite_ref-Weir1995p50_103-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Weir1995p50-103">&#91;103&#93;</a></sup> Many saw it as a punishment from God when Henry was later struck down with <a href="/wiki/Leprosy" title="Leprosy">leprosy</a> and <a href="/wiki/Epilepsy" title="Epilepsy">epilepsy</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-104" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-104">&#91;104&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h4><span id="Henry_V_.281413.E2.80.9321.29"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Henry_V_(1413\xe2\x80\x9321)">Henry V (1413\xe2\x80\x9321)</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=England_in_the_Late_Middle_Ages&amp;action=edit&amp;section=25" title="Edit section: Henry V (1413\xe2\x80\x9321)">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Henry_V_of_England" title="Henry V of England">Henry V of England</a> and <a href="/wiki/Hundred_Years%27_War_(1415%E2%80%931453)" class="mw-redirect" title="Hundred Years&#39; War (1415\xe2\x80\x931453)">Hundred Years\' War (1415\xe2\x80\x931453)</a></div>\n<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Hundred_years_war.gif" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e0/Hundred_years_war.gif/220px-Hundred_years_war.gif" width="220" height="253" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e0/Hundred_years_war.gif/330px-Hundred_years_war.gif 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e0/Hundred_years_war.gif/440px-Hundred_years_war.gif 2x" data-file-width="541" data-file-height="621" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Hundred_years_war.gif" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Hundred Years\' War evolution. French territory: yellow; English: grey; Burgundian: dark grey.</div></div></div>\n<p>Henry IV died in 1413. His son and successor, <a href="/wiki/Henry_V_of_England" title="Henry V of England">Henry V</a> was a successful and ruthless martial leader. Aware that <a href="/wiki/Charles_VI_of_France" title="Charles VI of France">Charles VI of France</a>\'s mental illness had caused instability in France, he invaded to assert the Plantagenet claims, captured <a href="/wiki/Harfleur" title="Harfleur">Harfleur</a>, made a chevauch\xc3\xa9e to Calais and won a near total victory over the French at the <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Agincourt" title="Battle of Agincourt">Battle of Agincourt</a> on 25 October 1415, despite being outnumbered, outmanoeuvred and low on supplies.<sup id="cite_ref-Schama265_105-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Schama265-105">&#91;105&#93;</a></sup> In subsequent years Henry recaptured much of Normandy and successfully secured marriage to <a href="/wiki/Catherine_of_Valois" title="Catherine of Valois">Catherine of Valois</a>. The resulting <a href="/wiki/Treaty_of_Troyes" title="Treaty of Troyes">Treaty of Troyes</a> stated that Henry\'s heirs would inherit the throne of France. However, conflict continued with the <a href="/wiki/Charles_VII_of_France" title="Charles VII of France">Dauphin</a>, and Henry\'s brother <a href="/wiki/Thomas,_Duke_of_Clarence" class="mw-redirect" title="Thomas, Duke of Clarence">Thomas, Duke of Clarence</a>, was killed in the defeat at the <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Baug%C3%A9" title="Battle of Baug\xc3\xa9">Battle of Baug\xc3\xa9</a> in 1421. When Henry died in 1422, possibly with dysentery, he was succeeded by his nine-month-old son as <a href="/wiki/Henry_VI_of_England" title="Henry VI of England">Henry VI of England</a>. The elderly Charles VI of France died two months later.<sup id="cite_ref-Davies76_106-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Davies76-106">&#91;106&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h4><span id="Henry_VI_.281421.E2.80.9371.29"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Henry_VI_(1421\xe2\x80\x9371)">Henry VI (1421\xe2\x80\x9371)</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=England_in_the_Late_Middle_Ages&amp;action=edit&amp;section=26" title="Edit section: Henry VI (1421\xe2\x80\x9371)">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Henry_VI_of_England" title="Henry VI of England">Henry VI of England</a></div>\n<p>Led by Henry\'s brother <a href="/wiki/John_of_Lancaster,_1st_Duke_of_Bedford" title="John of Lancaster, 1st Duke of Bedford">John of Lancaster, 1st Duke of Bedford</a>, there were several more English victories, such as the <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Verneuil" title="Battle of Verneuil">Battle of Verneuil</a>, in 1424, but it was impossible to maintain campaigning at this level. <a href="/wiki/Joan_of_Arc" title="Joan of Arc">Joan of Arc</a>\'s involvement helped force the lifting of the siege of Orleans.<sup id="cite_ref-Davies76_106-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Davies76-106">&#91;106&#93;</a></sup> French victory at the <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Patay" title="Battle of Patay">Battle of Patay</a> enabled the Dauphin to be crowned at <a href="/wiki/Reims" title="Reims">Reims</a> and continue the successful <a href="/wiki/Fabian_tactics" class="mw-redirect" title="Fabian tactics">Fabian tactics</a>, avoiding full frontal assaults and exploiting logistical advantage. Joan was captured by the Burgundians, sold to the English, tried as a witch and burned at the stake.<sup id="cite_ref-Weir1995p82_107-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Weir1995p82-107">&#91;107&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>During the <a href="/wiki/Regency_government,_1422%E2%80%931437" title="Regency government, 1422\xe2\x80\x931437">minority of Henry VI</a> the war caused political division amongst the legitimate and illegitimate Plantagenets. Bedford wanted to defend Normandy, <a href="/wiki/Humphrey_of_Lancaster,_1st_Duke_of_Gloucester" class="mw-redirect" title="Humphrey of Lancaster, 1st Duke of Gloucester">Humphrey of Lancaster, 1st Duke of Gloucester</a>, just Calais, but <a href="/wiki/Cardinal_Beaufort" class="mw-redirect" title="Cardinal Beaufort">Cardinal Beaufort</a> wanted peace.<sup id="cite_ref-Weir1995p72_108-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Weir1995p72-108">&#91;108&#93;</a></sup> This division led to Humphrey\'s wife being accused of using witchcraft with the aim of putting him on the throne. Humphrey was later arrested and died in prison.<sup id="cite_ref-Weir1995p122_109-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Weir1995p122-109">&#91;109&#93;</a></sup> The refusal to renounce the Plantagenet claim to the French crown at the <a href="/wiki/Treaty_of_Arras_(1435)" class="mw-redirect" title="Treaty of Arras (1435)">congress of Arras</a> enabled the former Plantagenet ally <a href="/wiki/Philip_III,_Duke_of_Burgundy" class="mw-redirect" title="Philip III, Duke of Burgundy">Philip III, Duke of Burgundy</a>, to reconcile with Charles, while giving Charles time to reorganise his feudal levies into a modern professional army that would put its superior numbers to good use.<sup id="cite_ref-Weir1995p86_110-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Weir1995p86-110">&#91;110&#93;</a></sup> The French retook Rouen and Bordeaux, regained Normandy, won the <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Formigny" title="Battle of Formigny">Battle of Formigny</a> in 1450<sup id="cite_ref-Weir1995p156_111-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Weir1995p156-111">&#91;111&#93;</a></sup> and, with victory at the <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Castillon" title="Battle of Castillon">Battle of Castillon</a> in 1453, brought an end to the war, leaving the English with only Calais and its surrounding Pale in continental France.<sup id="cite_ref-Weir1995p172_112-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Weir1995p172-112">&#91;112&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Henry VI was a weak king, and has been seen vulnerable to the over-mighty subjects created by the decline into <a href="/wiki/Bastard_feudalism" title="Bastard feudalism">bastard feudalism</a>, who took advantage of the <a href="/wiki/Feudal_levy" class="mw-redirect" title="Feudal levy">feudal levy</a> being replaced by taxation to develop private armies of liveried retainers. The result was rivalries that often spilled over from the courtroom into armed confrontations such as <a href="/wiki/Percy%E2%80%93Neville_feud" title="Percy\xe2\x80\x93Neville feud">Percy\xe2\x80\x93Neville feud</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Schama266_113-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Schama266-113">&#91;113&#93;</a></sup> The common interest given by the war in France had ended, so Richard, Duke of York, and <a href="/wiki/Richard_Neville,_16th_Earl_of_Warwick" title="Richard Neville, 16th Earl of Warwick">Richard Neville, 16th Earl of Warwick</a>, used their networks to defy the crown while the gentry attached themselves to different factions depending on their private feuds. Henry became the focus of discontent, as population, agricultural production, prices, wool trade and credit declined in the <a href="/wiki/Great_Slump_(15th_century)" title="Great Slump (15th century)">Great Slump</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Hicks44_114-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Hicks44-114">&#91;114&#93;</a></sup> Most seriously, in 1450 <a href="/wiki/Jack_Cade" class="mw-redirect" title="Jack Cade">Jack Cade</a> raised a rebellion in an attempt to force the king to address economic problems or abdicate his throne.<sup id="cite_ref-Weir147_115-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Weir147-115">&#91;115&#93;</a></sup> The uprising was suppressed, but the situation remained unsettled, with more radical demands coming from <a href="/wiki/John_and_William_Merfold" title="John and William Merfold">John and William Merfold</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Mate156_116-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Mate156-116">&#91;116&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Wars_of_the_Roses">Wars of the Roses</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=England_in_the_Late_Middle_Ages&amp;action=edit&amp;section=27" title="Edit section: Wars of the Roses">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Wars_of_the_Roses" title="Wars of the Roses">Wars of the Roses</a></div>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Battle_of_Barnet_retouched.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e9/Battle_of_Barnet_retouched.jpg/220px-Battle_of_Barnet_retouched.jpg" width="220" height="271" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e9/Battle_of_Barnet_retouched.jpg/330px-Battle_of_Barnet_retouched.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e9/Battle_of_Barnet_retouched.jpg/440px-Battle_of_Barnet_retouched.jpg 2x" data-file-width="775" data-file-height="955" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Battle_of_Barnet_retouched.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>A near-contemporary Flemish picture of the <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Barnet" title="Battle of Barnet">Battle of Barnet</a> in 1471</div></div></div>\n<p><a href="/wiki/Richard_Plantagenet,_Duke_of_York" class="mw-redirect" title="Richard Plantagenet, Duke of York">Richard Plantagenet, Duke of York</a>\'s attitude to the marriage contract of Henry and <a href="/wiki/Margaret_of_Anjou" title="Margaret of Anjou">Margaret of Anjou</a>, which included the surrender of Maine and extended the truce with France, contributed to his appointment as <a href="/wiki/Lord_Lieutenant_of_Ireland" title="Lord Lieutenant of Ireland">Lord Lieutenant of Ireland</a>. This conveniently removed him from English and French politics on which he had influence as a descendent of both Lionel, Duke of Clarence, and <a href="/wiki/Edmund_of_Langley" class="mw-redirect" title="Edmund of Langley">Edmund, Duke of York</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-croftonrichduke_117-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-croftonrichduke-117">&#91;117&#93;</a></sup> Conscious of the fate of Duke Humphrey at the hands of the Beauforts, and suspicious that Henry intended to nominate <a href="/wiki/Edmund_Beaufort,_2nd_Duke_of_Somerset" title="Edmund Beaufort, 2nd Duke of Somerset">Edmund Beaufort, 2nd Duke of Somerset</a>, as heir presumptive in his stead, he recruited militarily on his return to England. Richard claimed to be a reformer but was possibly plotting against his enemy Somerset. Armed conflict was avoided, because Richard lacked aristocratic support and was forced to swear allegiance to Henry. However, when Henry had a mental breakdown, Richard was named regent. Henry himself was trusting and not a man of war, but Margaret was more assertive, showing open enmity toward Richard, particularly after the birth of a male heir that resolved the succession question.<sup id="cite_ref-croftonrichdukeeleven_118-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-croftonrichdukeeleven-118">&#91;118&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>When Henry\'s sanity returned, the court party reasserted its authority. Richard of York and the Nevilles, who were related by marriage and had been alienated by Henry\'s support of the Percys, defeated them at a skirmish called the <a href="/wiki/First_Battle_of_St_Albans" title="First Battle of St Albans">First Battle of St Albans</a>. Possibly as few as 50 men were killed, but among them were Somerset and the two Percy lords, <a href="/wiki/Henry_Percy,_2nd_Earl_of_Northumberland" title="Henry Percy, 2nd Earl of Northumberland">Henry Percy, 2nd Earl of Northumberland</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Thomas_Clifford,_8th_Baron_de_Clifford" title="Thomas Clifford, 8th Baron de Clifford">Thomas Clifford, 8th Baron de Clifford</a>, creating feuds that would prove impossible to reconcile; reputedly Clifford\'s son would later murder Richard\'s son Edmund. The ruling class was deeply shocked and reconciliation was attempted.<sup id="cite_ref-119" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-119">&#91;119&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-120" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-120">&#91;120&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Threatened with treason charges and lacking support, York, <a href="/wiki/Richard_Neville,_5th_Earl_of_Salisbury" title="Richard Neville, 5th Earl of Salisbury">Richard Neville, 5th Earl of Salisbury</a>, and Richard Neville, 16th Earl of Warwick, fled abroad. The Nevilles returned to win the <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Northampton_(1460)" title="Battle of Northampton (1460)">Battle of Northampton</a>, where they captured Henry.<sup id="cite_ref-121" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-121">&#91;121&#93;</a></sup> When Richard joined them, he surprised Parliament by claiming the throne, then forcing through the <a href="/wiki/Act_of_Accord" title="Act of Accord">Act of Accord</a>, which stated that Henry would remain as monarch for his lifetime, but would be succeeded by York. Margaret found this disregarding of her son\'s claims unacceptable and so the conflict continued. York was killed at the <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Wakefield" title="Battle of Wakefield">Battle of Wakefield</a> and his head set on display at <a href="/wiki/Micklegate_Bar" class="mw-redirect" title="Micklegate Bar">Micklegate Bar</a>, along with those of his son <a href="/wiki/Edmund,_Earl_of_Rutland" title="Edmund, Earl of Rutland">Edmund, Earl of Rutland</a>, and Richard Neville, Earl of Salisbury, who had both been captured and beheaded.<sup id="cite_ref-Weir257_122-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Weir257-122">&#91;122&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="House_of_York">House of York</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=England_in_the_Late_Middle_Ages&amp;action=edit&amp;section=28" title="Edit section: House of York">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/House_of_York" title="House of York">House of York</a></div>\n<h4><span id="Edward_IV_.281461.E2.80.9383.29"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Edward_IV_(1461\xe2\x80\x9383)">Edward IV (1461\xe2\x80\x9383)</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=England_in_the_Late_Middle_Ages&amp;action=edit&amp;section=29" title="Edit section: Edward IV (1461\xe2\x80\x9383)">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Edward_IV_of_England" title="Edward IV of England">Edward IV of England</a></div>\n<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Copper-alloy_boar_mount_from_the_Thames_foreshore_(London).jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ea/Copper-alloy_boar_mount_from_the_Thames_foreshore_%28London%29.jpg/220px-Copper-alloy_boar_mount_from_the_Thames_foreshore_%28London%29.jpg" width="220" height="169" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ea/Copper-alloy_boar_mount_from_the_Thames_foreshore_%28London%29.jpg/330px-Copper-alloy_boar_mount_from_the_Thames_foreshore_%28London%29.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ea/Copper-alloy_boar_mount_from_the_Thames_foreshore_%28London%29.jpg/440px-Copper-alloy_boar_mount_from_the_Thames_foreshore_%28London%29.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1337" data-file-height="1027" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Copper-alloy_boar_mount_from_the_Thames_foreshore_(London).jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Bronze" title="Bronze">Bronze</a> <a href="/wiki/Boar" class="mw-redirect" title="Boar">boar</a> <a href="/wiki/Livery_badge" class="mw-redirect" title="Livery badge">livery badge</a> thought to have been worn by a supporter of Richard III, often described as the last Plantagenet king</div></div></div>\n<p>The Scottish queen <a href="/wiki/Mary_of_Guelders" title="Mary of Guelders">Mary of Guelders</a> provided Margaret with support and a Scottish army pillaged into southern England.<sup id="cite_ref-123" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-123">&#91;123&#93;</a></sup> London resisted in the fear of being plundered, then enthusiastically welcomed York\'s son <a href="/wiki/Edward_IV_of_England" title="Edward IV of England">Edward, Earl of March</a>, with Parliament confirming that Edward should be made king.<sup id="cite_ref-crowned_124-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-crowned-124">&#91;124&#93;</a></sup> Edward was crowned as Edward IV after consolidating his position with victory at the <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Towton" title="Battle of Towton">Battle of Towton</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-125" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-125">&#91;125&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Edward\'s preferment of the former Lancastrian-supporting Woodville family, following his marriage to <a href="/wiki/Elizabeth_Woodville" title="Elizabeth Woodville">Elizabeth Woodville</a>, led to Warwick and Edward\'s brother <a href="/wiki/George_Plantagenet,_1st_Duke_of_Clarence" title="George Plantagenet, 1st Duke of Clarence">George Duke of Clarence</a> helping Margaret depose Edward and return Henry to the throne in 1470.<sup id="cite_ref-126" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-126">&#91;126&#93;</a></sup> Edward and his brother <a href="/wiki/Richard_III_of_England" title="Richard III of England">Richard, Duke of Gloucester</a>, fled, but on their return the next year Clarence switched sides at the <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Barnet" title="Battle of Barnet">Battle of Barnet</a>, leading to the death of the Neville brothers.<sup id="cite_ref-127" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-127">&#91;127&#93;</a></sup> The subsequent <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Tewkesbury" title="Battle of Tewkesbury">Battle of Tewkesbury</a> brought the demise of the last of the male line of the Beauforts. The battlefield execution of <a href="/wiki/Edward_of_Westminster,_Prince_of_Wales" title="Edward of Westminster, Prince of Wales">Edward of Westminster, Prince of Wales</a>, and later murder of Henry VI extinguished the House of Lancaster.<sup id="cite_ref-128" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-128">&#91;128&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h4><span id="Edward_V_and_Richard_III_.281483.E2.80.9385.29"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Edward_V_and_Richard_III_(1483\xe2\x80\x9385)">Edward V and Richard III (1483\xe2\x80\x9385)</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=England_in_the_Late_Middle_Ages&amp;action=edit&amp;section=30" title="Edit section: Edward V and Richard III (1483\xe2\x80\x9385)">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Richard_III_of_England" title="Richard III of England">Richard III of England</a></div><p>\nBy the mid-1470s, the victorious House of York looked safely established, with seven living male princes, but it quickly brought about its own demise. Clarence plotted against his brother and was executed. Following Edward\'s premature death in 1483, his son Edward Prince of Wales was declared king, but his uncle Richard had Parliament declare him and his brother <a href="/wiki/Richard_of_Shrewsbury,_Duke_of_York" title="Richard of Shrewsbury, Duke of York">Richard, Duke of York</a> illegitimate on the pretext of an alleged prior pre-contract to <a href="/wiki/Lady_Eleanor_Talbot" title="Lady Eleanor Talbot">Lady Eleanor Talbot</a>, leaving Edward\'s marriage invalid.<sup id="cite_ref-titreg_129-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-titreg-129">&#91;129&#93;</a></sup> Richard seized the throne and Edward\'s sons, the <a href="/wiki/Princes_in_the_Tower" title="Princes in the Tower">Princes in the Tower</a>, were never seen again. Richard\'s son predeceased him. In 1485 there was an invasion of foreign mercenaries led by <a href="/wiki/Henry_VII_of_England" title="Henry VII of England">Henry Tudor</a>, who claimed the throne through his mother <a href="/wiki/Margaret_Beaufort,_Countess_of_Richmond_and_Derby" class="mw-redirect" title="Margaret Beaufort, Countess of Richmond and Derby">Margaret Beaufort</a>. After Richard was killed at the <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Bosworth_Field" title="Battle of Bosworth Field">Battle of Bosworth Field</a>, Tudor assumed the throne as Henry VII, founding the Tudor dynasty and bringing the Plantagenet line of kings to an end.<sup id="cite_ref-autogenerated2_130-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-autogenerated2-130">&#91;130&#93;</a></sup></p><div style="clear:both;"></div>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Government_and_society">Government and society</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=England_in_the_Late_Middle_Ages&amp;action=edit&amp;section=31" title="Edit section: Government and society">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Governance_and_social_structures">Governance and social structures</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=England_in_the_Late_Middle_Ages&amp;action=edit&amp;section=32" title="Edit section: Governance and social structures">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Social_history_of_England#Late_medieval_society" class="mw-redirect" title="Social history of England">Social history of the Late Middle Ages</a></div>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Edward_III_of_England_(Order_of_the_Garter).jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/de/Edward_III_of_England_%28Order_of_the_Garter%29.jpg/170px-Edward_III_of_England_%28Order_of_the_Garter%29.jpg" width="170" height="231" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/de/Edward_III_of_England_%28Order_of_the_Garter%29.jpg/255px-Edward_III_of_England_%28Order_of_the_Garter%29.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/de/Edward_III_of_England_%28Order_of_the_Garter%29.jpg/340px-Edward_III_of_England_%28Order_of_the_Garter%29.jpg 2x" data-file-width="648" data-file-height="880" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Edward_III_of_England_(Order_of_the_Garter).jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Early fifteenth-century depiction of <a href="/wiki/Edward_III_of_England" title="Edward III of England">Edward III</a>, shown wearing the <a href="/wiki/Chivalric" class="mw-redirect" title="Chivalric">chivalric</a> symbols of the <a href="/wiki/Order_of_the_Garter" title="Order of the Garter">Order of the Garter</a></div></div></div>\n<p>On becoming king in 1272, Edward I reestablished royal power, overhauling the royal finances and appealing to the broader English elite by using Parliament to authorise the raising of new taxes and to hear petitions concerning abuses of local governance.<sup id="cite_ref-131" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-131">&#91;131&#93;</a></sup> This political balance collapsed under Edward II and savage civil wars broke out during the 1320s.<sup id="cite_ref-132" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-132">&#91;132&#93;</a></sup> Edward III restored order once more with the help of a majority of the nobility, exercising power through the <a href="/wiki/Exchequer" title="Exchequer">exchequer</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Court_of_Common_Pleas_(England)" title="Court of Common Pleas (England)">common bench</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Royal_Households_of_the_United_Kingdom" title="Royal Households of the United Kingdom">royal household</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-133" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-133">&#91;133&#93;</a></sup> This government was better organised and on a larger scale than ever before, and by the fourteenth century the king\'s formerly peripatetic <a href="/wiki/Chancery_(medieval_office)" title="Chancery (medieval office)">chancery</a> had to take up permanent residence in <a href="/wiki/Westminster" title="Westminster">Westminster</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-134" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-134">&#91;134&#93;</a></sup> Edward used Parliament even more than his predecessors to handle general administration, to legislate and to raise the necessary taxes to pay for the wars in France.<sup id="cite_ref-135" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-135">&#91;135&#93;</a></sup> The royal lands\xe2\x80\x94and incomes from them\xe2\x80\x94had diminished over the years, and increasingly frequent taxation was required to support royal initiatives.<sup id="cite_ref-136" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-136">&#91;136&#93;</a></sup> Edward held elaborate chivalric events in an effort to unite his supporters around the symbols of knighthood.<sup id="cite_ref-137" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-137">&#91;137&#93;</a></sup> The ideal of <a href="/wiki/Chivalry" title="Chivalry">chivalry</a> continued to develop throughout the fourteenth century, reflected in the growth of <a href="/wiki/Chivalric_order" class="mw-redirect" title="Chivalric order">knightly orders</a> (including the <a href="/wiki/Order_of_the_Garter" title="Order of the Garter">Order of the Garter</a>), grand <a href="/wiki/Tournament" title="Tournament">tournaments</a> and <a href="/wiki/Round_Table_(tournament)" title="Round Table (tournament)">round table</a> events.<sup id="cite_ref-138" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-138">&#91;138&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>By the time that Richard II was deposed in 1399, the power of the major noble magnates had grown considerably; powerful rulers such as Henry IV would contain them, but during the minority of Henry VI they controlled the country.<sup id="cite_ref-139" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-139">&#91;139&#93;</a></sup> The magnates depended upon their income from rent and trade to allow them to maintain groups of paid, armed retainers, often sporting controversial liveries, and to buy support amongst the wider gentry; this system has been dubbed <a href="/wiki/Bastard_feudalism" title="Bastard feudalism">bastard feudalism</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-140" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-140">&#91;140&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-142" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-142">&#91;nb 1&#93;</a></sup> Their influence was exerted both through the <a href="/wiki/House_of_Lords" title="House of Lords">House of Lords</a> at Parliament and through the king\'s council.<sup id="cite_ref-143" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-143">&#91;142&#93;</a></sup> The gentry and wealthier townsmen exercised increasing influence through the <a href="/wiki/House_of_Commons_of_England" title="House of Commons of England">House of Commons</a>, opposing raising taxes to pay for the French wars.<sup id="cite_ref-144" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-144">&#91;143&#93;</a></sup> Im the 1430s and 1440s the English government was in major financial difficulties, leading to the crisis of 1450 and a popular revolt under the leadership of Jack Cade.<sup id="cite_ref-145" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-145">&#91;144&#93;</a></sup> Law and order deteriorated, and the crown was unable to intervene in the factional fighting between different nobles and their followers.<sup id="cite_ref-146" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-146">&#91;145&#93;</a></sup> The resulting Wars of the Roses saw a savage escalation of violence between the noble leaderships of both sides: captured enemies were executed and family lands <a href="/wiki/Attainted" class="mw-redirect" title="Attainted">attainted</a>. By the time that Henry VII took the throne in 1485, England\'s governmental and social structures had been substantially weakened, with whole noble lines extinguished.<sup id="cite_ref-147" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-147">&#91;146&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Women">Women</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=England_in_the_Late_Middle_Ages&amp;action=edit&amp;section=33" title="Edit section: Women">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Women_in_the_Middle_Ages" title="Women in the Middle Ages">Women in the Middle Ages</a></div>\n<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Elizabeth_of_Suffolk.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/50/Elizabeth_of_Suffolk.jpg/220px-Elizabeth_of_Suffolk.jpg" width="220" height="246" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/50/Elizabeth_of_Suffolk.jpg/330px-Elizabeth_of_Suffolk.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/50/Elizabeth_of_Suffolk.jpg/440px-Elizabeth_of_Suffolk.jpg 2x" data-file-width="447" data-file-height="500" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Elizabeth_of_Suffolk.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Tomb of <a href="/wiki/Elizabeth_of_York,_Duchess_of_Suffolk" title="Elizabeth of York, Duchess of Suffolk">Elizabeth of York, Duchess of Suffolk</a> at <a href="/wiki/Wingfield,_Suffolk" title="Wingfield, Suffolk">Wingfield, Suffolk</a> (built 1503)</div></div></div>\n<p>Medieval England was a <a href="/wiki/Patriarchy" title="Patriarchy">patriarchal society</a> and the lives of women were heavily influenced by contemporary beliefs about gender and authority.<sup id="cite_ref-148" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-148">&#91;147&#93;</a></sup> However, the position of women varied considerably according to various factors, including their <a href="/wiki/Social_class" title="Social class">social class</a>; whether they were unmarried, married, widowed or remarried; and in which part of the country they lived.<sup id="cite_ref-149" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-149">&#91;148&#93;</a></sup> Significant gender inequities persisted throughout the period, as women typically had more limited life-choices, access to employment and trade, and legal rights than men.<sup id="cite_ref-150" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-150">&#91;149&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>The growth of governmental institutions under a succession of bishops reduced the role of queens and their households in formal government. Married or widowed noblewomen remained significant cultural and religious patrons and played an important part in political and military events, even if chroniclers were uncertain if this was appropriate behaviour.<sup id="cite_ref-151" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-151">&#91;150&#93;</a></sup> As in earlier centuries, most women worked in agriculture, but here roles became more clearly gendered, with <a href="/wiki/Ploughing" class="mw-redirect" title="Ploughing">ploughing</a> and managing the fields defined as men\'s work, for example, and <a href="/wiki/Dairy_farming" title="Dairy farming">dairy production</a> becoming dominated by women.<sup id="cite_ref-152" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-152">&#91;151&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>The years after the Black Death left many women widows; in the wider economy labour was in short supply and land was suddenly readily available.<sup id="cite_ref-153" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-153">&#91;152&#93;</a></sup> In rural areas peasant women could enjoy a better standard of living than ever before, but the amount of work being done by women may have increased.<sup id="cite_ref-154" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-154">&#91;153&#93;</a></sup> Many other women travelled to the towns and cities, to the point where they outnumbered men in some settlements.<sup id="cite_ref-155" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-155">&#91;154&#93;</a></sup> There they worked with their husbands, or in a limited number of occupations, including <a href="/wiki/Spinning_(textiles)" title="Spinning (textiles)">spinning</a>, making clothes, <a href="/wiki/Victualling" class="mw-redirect" title="Victualling">victualling</a> and as servants.<sup id="cite_ref-156" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-156">&#91;155&#93;</a></sup> Some women became full-time <a href="/wiki/Ale" title="Ale">ale</a> brewers, until they were pushed out of business by the male-dominated <a href="/wiki/Beer" title="Beer">beer</a> industry in the fifteenth century.<sup id="cite_ref-157" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-157">&#91;156&#93;</a></sup> Higher status jobs and apprenticeships, however, remained closed to women.<sup id="cite_ref-158" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-158">&#91;157&#93;</a></sup> As in earlier times, noblewomen exercised power on their estates in their husbands\' absence and again, if necessary, defended them in sieges and skirmishes.<sup id="cite_ref-159" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-159">&#91;158&#93;</a></sup> Wealthy widows who could successfully claim their rightful share of their late husband\'s property could live as powerful members of the community in their own right.<sup id="cite_ref-160" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-160">&#91;159&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Identity">Identity</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=England_in_the_Late_Middle_Ages&amp;action=edit&amp;section=34" title="Edit section: Identity">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/English_national_identity" title="English national identity">English national identity</a></div>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Castell_de_Windsor_-_Capella_de_Sant_Jordi.JPG" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/80/Castell_de_Windsor_-_Capella_de_Sant_Jordi.JPG/220px-Castell_de_Windsor_-_Capella_de_Sant_Jordi.JPG" width="220" height="146" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/80/Castell_de_Windsor_-_Capella_de_Sant_Jordi.JPG/330px-Castell_de_Windsor_-_Capella_de_Sant_Jordi.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/80/Castell_de_Windsor_-_Capella_de_Sant_Jordi.JPG/440px-Castell_de_Windsor_-_Capella_de_Sant_Jordi.JPG 2x" data-file-width="3008" data-file-height="2000" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Castell_de_Windsor_-_Capella_de_Sant_Jordi.JPG" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The <a href="/wiki/English_Gothic_architecture" title="English Gothic architecture">English Gothic</a> <a href="/wiki/Vault_(architecture)" title="Vault (architecture)">vaulted ceiling</a> of <a href="/wiki/St_George%27s_Chapel,_Windsor_Castle" title="St George&#39;s Chapel, Windsor Castle">St George\'s Chapel</a>, <a href="/wiki/Windsor_Castle" title="Windsor Castle">Windsor Castle</a></div></div></div>\n<p>During the twelfth and thirteenth centuries, the English began to consider themselves superior to the Welsh, Scots and <a href="/wiki/Bretons" title="Bretons">Bretons</a>. They perceived themselves as civilised, economically prosperous and properly Christian, while the <a href="/wiki/Celtic_fringe" class="mw-redirect" title="Celtic fringe">Celtic fringe</a> was considered lazy, barbarous and backward.<sup id="cite_ref-161" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-161">&#91;160&#93;</a></sup> Following the <a href="/wiki/Norman_invasion_of_Ireland" title="Norman invasion of Ireland">invasion of Ireland</a> in the late twelfth century, similar feelings were expressed about the Irish, with the distinctions clarified and reinforced in fourteenth-century English legislation.<sup id="cite_ref-162" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-162">&#91;161&#93;</a></sup> The English also felt strongly about the foreign traders who lived in the special enclaves in London in the Late Middle Ages; there was considerable hostility to Jews, resulting in their expulsion, but Italian and Baltic traders were also regarded as aliens and were frequently the targets of violence during economic downturns.<sup id="cite_ref-Hicks,_pp._52-53_163-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Hicks,_pp._52-53-163">&#91;162&#93;</a></sup> Even within England, different identities abounded, each with their own sense of status and importance. Regional identities could be significant \xe2\x80\x93 men and women from <a href="/wiki/Yorkshire" title="Yorkshire">Yorkshire</a>, for example, had a clear identity within English society, and professional groups with a distinct identity, such as lawyers, engaged in open fighting with others in cities such as London.<sup id="cite_ref-164" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-164">&#91;163&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>English began to be used as a second language of the court during the reign of Edward I.<sup id="cite_ref-Dolan2001p209_165-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Dolan2001p209-165">&#91;164&#93;</a></sup> Edward III encouraged the re-adoption of English as the official language of courts and parliament, with the <a href="/wiki/Pleading_in_English_Act_1362" title="Pleading in English Act 1362">Statute of Pleading</a> establishing English as the language of royal and <a href="/wiki/Seignorial_court" class="mw-redirect" title="Seignorial court">seignorial courts</a> and it was officially adopted for diplomatic language in place of French in the reign of Henry IV.<sup id="cite_ref-Dolan2001p209_165-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Dolan2001p209-165">&#91;164&#93;</a></sup> The Hundred Years War has been seen by a number of scholars as important in creating an English national identity, evidenced in its use of propaganda, the growth of cultural slurs and national stereotypes, intense intellectual debate between scholars of different kingdoms, cartographic evidence that indicated national boundaries, the role of the patron <a href="/wiki/Saint_George" title="Saint George">Saint George</a> and of the Church in delivering a central message. It saw a change in the composition of armies, with the lower ranks identifying with a national cause and responding to a call to arms and the development of near permanent taxation that made the general population investors in a national enterprise. There was also the growth of chivalric orders like that of the Garter and the increasing central role of the monarchy and parliament in English life.<sup id="cite_ref-166" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-166">&#91;165&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Jews_and_the_expulsion">Jews and the expulsion</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=England_in_the_Late_Middle_Ages&amp;action=edit&amp;section=35" title="Edit section: Jews and the expulsion">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/History_of_the_Jews_in_England_(1066%E2%80%931290)#Increasing_persecution,_13th_century" title="History of the Jews in England (1066\xe2\x80\x931290)">History of the Jews in England (1066\xe2\x80\x931290) \xc2\xa7&#160;Increasing persecution, 13th century</a>; and <a href="/wiki/Edict_of_Expulsion" title="Edict of Expulsion">Edict of Expulsion</a></div>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Religion">Religion</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=England_in_the_Late_Middle_Ages&amp;action=edit&amp;section=36" title="Edit section: Religion">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Religion_in_Medieval_England" title="Religion in Medieval England">Religion in Medieval England</a></div>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Religious_institutions">Religious institutions</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=England_in_the_Late_Middle_Ages&amp;action=edit&amp;section=37" title="Edit section: Religious institutions">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:English_-_Pilgrimage_Flask_from_the_Shrine_of_Thomas_Becket_in_Canterbury_-_Walters_5599_-_View_B.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9e/English_-_Pilgrimage_Flask_from_the_Shrine_of_Thomas_Becket_in_Canterbury_-_Walters_5599_-_View_B.jpg/220px-English_-_Pilgrimage_Flask_from_the_Shrine_of_Thomas_Becket_in_Canterbury_-_Walters_5599_-_View_B.jpg" width="220" height="240" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9e/English_-_Pilgrimage_Flask_from_the_Shrine_of_Thomas_Becket_in_Canterbury_-_Walters_5599_-_View_B.jpg/330px-English_-_Pilgrimage_Flask_from_the_Shrine_of_Thomas_Becket_in_Canterbury_-_Walters_5599_-_View_B.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9e/English_-_Pilgrimage_Flask_from_the_Shrine_of_Thomas_Becket_in_Canterbury_-_Walters_5599_-_View_B.jpg/440px-English_-_Pilgrimage_Flask_from_the_Shrine_of_Thomas_Becket_in_Canterbury_-_Walters_5599_-_View_B.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1185" data-file-height="1295" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:English_-_Pilgrimage_Flask_from_the_Shrine_of_Thomas_Becket_in_Canterbury_-_Walters_5599_-_View_B.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>A <a href="/wiki/Pilgrim" title="Pilgrim">pilgrim</a>\'s flask, carried as a protective talisman, containing <a href="/wiki/Holy_water" title="Holy water">holy water</a> from the shrine of Thomas Becket in <a href="/wiki/Canterbury_Cathedral" title="Canterbury Cathedral">Canterbury Cathedral</a></div></div></div>\n<p>New religious orders began to be introduced into England in this period. The French <a href="/wiki/Cluniac_order" class="mw-redirect" title="Cluniac order">Cluniac order</a> became fashionable and their houses were introduced in England from the late eleventh century.<sup id="cite_ref-167" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-167">&#91;166&#93;</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Augustinians" title="Augustinians">Augustinians</a> spread quickly from the beginning of the twelfth century, while later in the century the <a href="/wiki/Cistercians" title="Cistercians">Cistercians</a> reached England, creating houses with a more austere interpretation of monastic rules and building the great abbeys of <a href="/wiki/Rievaulx_Abbey" title="Rievaulx Abbey">Rievaulx</a> and <a href="/wiki/Fountains_Abbey" title="Fountains Abbey">Fountains</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-168" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-168">&#91;167&#93;</a></sup> By 1215, there were over 600 monastic communities in England, but new endowments slowed during the thirteenth century, creating long-term financial problems for many institutions.<sup id="cite_ref-169" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-169">&#91;168&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Dominican_Order" title="Dominican Order">Dominican</a> and <a href="/wiki/Franciscan" class="mw-redirect" title="Franciscan">Franciscan</a> <a href="/wiki/Friar" title="Friar">friars</a> arrived in England during the 1220s, establishing 150 friaries by the end of the thirteenth century; these <a href="/wiki/Mendicant" title="Mendicant">mendicant</a> orders rapidly became popular, particularly in towns, and heavily influenced local preaching.<sup id="cite_ref-170" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-170">&#91;169&#93;</a></sup> The religious <a href="/wiki/Military_order_(society)" class="mw-redirect" title="Military order (society)">military orders</a> that became popular across Europe from the twelfth century acquired possessions in England, including the <a href="/wiki/Templars" class="mw-redirect" title="Templars">Templars</a>, <a href="/wiki/Teutonic_Order" title="Teutonic Order">Teutonic Order</a> and <a href="/wiki/Hospitallers" class="mw-redirect" title="Hospitallers">Hospitallers</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-171" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-171">&#91;170&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Pilgrimage">Pilgrimage</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=England_in_the_Late_Middle_Ages&amp;action=edit&amp;section=38" title="Edit section: Pilgrimage">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<p><a href="/wiki/Pilgrimage" title="Pilgrimage">Pilgrimages</a> were a popular religious practice throughout the Middle Ages in England.<sup id="cite_ref-172" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-172">&#91;171&#93;</a></sup> Typically pilgrims would travel short distances to a shrine or a particular church, either to do <a href="/wiki/Penance" title="Penance">penance</a> for a perceived sin, or to <a href="/wiki/Faith_healing" title="Faith healing">seek relief</a> from an illness or other condition.<sup id="cite_ref-173" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-173">&#91;172&#93;</a></sup> Some pilgrims travelled further, either to more distant sites within Britain or, in a few cases, onto the continent.<sup id="cite_ref-174" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-174">&#91;173&#93;</a></sup> Major shrines in the late Middle Ages included those of <a href="/wiki/Thomas_Becket" title="Thomas Becket">Thomas Becket</a> at <a href="/wiki/Canterbury_Cathedral" title="Canterbury Cathedral">Canterbury</a>, <a href="/wiki/Edward_the_Confessor" title="Edward the Confessor">Edward the Confessor</a>, at <a href="/wiki/Westminster_Abbey" title="Westminster Abbey">Westminster Abbey</a>, <a href="/wiki/Hugh_of_Lincoln" title="Hugh of Lincoln">Hugh of Lincoln</a>, <a href="/wiki/St._William_of_York" class="mw-redirect" title="St. William of York">William of York</a>, <a href="/wiki/Edmund_Rich" class="mw-redirect" title="Edmund Rich">Edmund Rich</a>, Archbishop of Canterbury, who was buried at <a href="/wiki/Pontigny_Abbey" title="Pontigny Abbey">Pontigny Abbey</a> in France, <a href="/wiki/Richard_of_Chichester" title="Richard of Chichester">Richard of Chichester</a>, <a href="/wiki/Thomas_de_Cantilupe" title="Thomas de Cantilupe">Thomas Cantilupe</a> of <a href="/wiki/Hereford_Cathedral" title="Hereford Cathedral">Hereford</a>, <a href="/wiki/Osmund_(bishop_of_Salisbury)" class="mw-redirect" title="Osmund (bishop of Salisbury)">St Osmund of Salisbury</a> and <a href="/wiki/John_of_Bridlington" class="mw-redirect" title="John of Bridlington">John of Bridlington</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-175" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-175">&#91;174&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Heresy">Heresy</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=England_in_the_Late_Middle_Ages&amp;action=edit&amp;section=39" title="Edit section: Heresy">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Church_and_state_in_medieval_Europe" title="Church and state in medieval Europe">Church and state in medieval Europe</a></div><p>\nIn the 1380s several challenges emerged to the traditional teachings of the Church, resulting from the teachings of <a href="/wiki/John_Wycliffe" title="John Wycliffe">John Wycliffe</a>, a member of <a href="/wiki/Oxford_University" class="mw-redirect" title="Oxford University">Oxford University</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-176" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-176">&#91;175&#93;</a></sup> Wycliffe argued that <a href="/wiki/Scripture" class="mw-redirect" title="Scripture">scripture</a> was the best guide to understanding God\'s intentions, and that the superficial nature of the <a href="/wiki/Liturgy" title="Liturgy">liturgy</a>, combined with the abuses of wealth within the Church and the role of senior churchmen in government, distracted from that study.<sup id="cite_ref-177" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-177">&#91;176&#93;</a></sup> A loose movement that included many members of the gentry pursued these ideas after Wycliffe\'s death in 1384 and attempted to pass a <a href="/wiki/Act_of_Parliament" title="Act of Parliament">Parliamentary bill</a> in 1395: the movement was rapidly condemned by the authorities and was termed "<a href="/wiki/Lollardy" title="Lollardy">Lollardy</a>".<sup id="cite_ref-178" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-178">&#91;177&#93;</a></sup> The English bishops were charged with controlling and countering this trend, disrupting Lollard preachers and to enforcing the teaching of suitable sermons in local churches.<sup id="cite_ref-179" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-179">&#91;178&#93;</a></sup> By the early fifteenth century, combating Lollard teachings had become a key political issue, championed by Henry IV and his Lancastrian followers, who used the powers of both church and state to combat the <a href="/wiki/Heresy" title="Heresy">heresy</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-180" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-180">&#91;179&#93;</a></sup></p><div style="clear:both;"></div>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Economy_and_technology">Economy and technology</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=England_in_the_Late_Middle_Ages&amp;action=edit&amp;section=40" title="Edit section: Economy and technology">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Geography">Geography</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=England_in_the_Late_Middle_Ages&amp;action=edit&amp;section=41" title="Edit section: Geography">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Geography_of_England" title="Geography of England">Geography of England</a></div>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Medieval_Hunting_Park.JPG" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9e/Medieval_Hunting_Park.JPG/170px-Medieval_Hunting_Park.JPG" width="170" height="258" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9e/Medieval_Hunting_Park.JPG/255px-Medieval_Hunting_Park.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9e/Medieval_Hunting_Park.JPG/340px-Medieval_Hunting_Park.JPG 2x" data-file-width="674" data-file-height="1024" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Medieval_Hunting_Park.JPG" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Fifteenth-century depiction of an English <a href="/wiki/Medieval_deer_park" class="mw-redirect" title="Medieval deer park">hunting park</a></div></div></div>\n<p>England had a diverse geography in the medieval period, from the <a href="/wiki/Fenlands" class="mw-redirect" title="Fenlands">Fenlands</a> of <a href="/wiki/East_Anglia" title="East Anglia">East Anglia</a> and the heavily wooded <a href="/wiki/Weald" title="Weald">Weald</a>, through to the upland <a href="/wiki/Moorland" title="Moorland">moors</a> of <a href="/wiki/Yorkshire" title="Yorkshire">Yorkshire</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-181" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-181">&#91;180&#93;</a></sup> Despite this, medieval England broadly formed two zones, roughly divided by the rivers <a href="/wiki/River_Exe" title="River Exe">Exe</a> and <a href="/wiki/River_Tees" title="River Tees">Tes</a>: the south and east of England had lighter, richer soils, able to support both <a href="/wiki/Arable_land" title="Arable land">arable</a> and <a href="/wiki/Pastoralism" title="Pastoralism">pastoral</a> agriculture, while the poorer soils and colder climate of the north and west produced a predominantly pastoral economy.<sup id="cite_ref-182" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-182">&#91;181&#93;</a></sup> Slightly more land was covered by trees than in the twentieth century, and <a href="/wiki/Bear" title="Bear">bears</a>, <a href="/wiki/Beaver" title="Beaver">beavers</a> and <a href="/wiki/Wolf" title="Wolf">wolves</a> lived wild in England, bears being hunted to extinction by the eleventh century and beavers by the twelfth.<sup id="cite_ref-Dyer2009p13_183-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Dyer2009p13-183">&#91;182&#93;</a></sup> Of the 10,000 miles of roads that had been built by the Romans, many remained in use and four were of particular strategic importance\xe2\x80\x94the <a href="/wiki/Icknield_Way" title="Icknield Way">Icknield Way</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Fosse_Way" title="Fosse Way">Fosse Way</a>, <a href="/wiki/Ermine_Street" title="Ermine Street">Ermine Street</a> and <a href="/wiki/Watling_Street" title="Watling Street">Watling Street</a>\xe2\x80\x94which criss-crossed the entire country.<sup id="cite_ref-184" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-184">&#91;183&#93;</a></sup> The road system was adequate for the needs of the period, although it was significantly cheaper to transport goods by water.<sup id="cite_ref-185" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-185">&#91;184&#93;</a></sup> The major river networks formed key transport routes, while many English towns formed navigable <a href="/wiki/Inland_port" title="Inland port">inland ports</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-186" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-186">&#91;185&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>For much of the Middle Ages, England\'s climate differed from that in the twenty-first century. Between the ninth and thirteenth centuries England went through the <a href="/wiki/Medieval_Warm_Period" title="Medieval Warm Period">Medieval Warm Period</a>, a prolonged ear of milder temperatures; in the early thirteenth century, for example, summers were around 1&#160;\xc2\xb0C warmer than today and the climate was slightly drier.<sup id="cite_ref-DanzigerGillinghamp33_187-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-DanzigerGillinghamp33-187">&#91;186&#93;</a></sup> These warmer temperatures allowed poorer land to be brought into cultivation and for <a href="/wiki/Grapevine" class="mw-redirect" title="Grapevine">grapevines</a> to be cultivated relatively far north.<sup id="cite_ref-DanzigerGillinghamp33_187-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-DanzigerGillinghamp33-187">&#91;186&#93;</a></sup> The Warm Period was followed by several centuries of much cooler temperatures, termed the <a href="/wiki/Little_Ice_Age" title="Little Ice Age">Little Ice Age</a>; by the fourteenth century spring temperatures had dropped considerably, reaching their coldest in the 1340s and 1350s.<sup id="cite_ref-188" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-188">&#91;187&#93;</a></sup> This cold end to the Middle Ages impacted significantly on English agriculture and living conditions.<sup id="cite_ref-189" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-189">&#91;188&#93;</a></sup> England\'s environment continued to be shaped throughout the period, through the building of <a href="/wiki/Ditch" title="Ditch">dykes</a> to drain marshes, tree clearance and the large scale extraction of <a href="/wiki/Peat" title="Peat">peat</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-190" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-190">&#91;189&#93;</a></sup> Managed <a href="/wiki/Medieval_deer_park" class="mw-redirect" title="Medieval deer park">parks</a> for hunting game, including deer and boars, were built as status symbols by the nobility from the twelfth century.<sup id="cite_ref-191" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-191">&#91;190&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Economy">Economy</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=England_in_the_Late_Middle_Ages&amp;action=edit&amp;section=42" title="Edit section: Economy">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Economy_of_England_in_the_Middle_Ages" title="Economy of England in the Middle Ages">Economy of England in the Middle Ages</a> and <a href="/wiki/Demography_of_England" title="Demography of England">Demography of England</a></div>\n<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Southampton_Medieval_Merchants_House_Hall.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e6/Southampton_Medieval_Merchants_House_Hall.jpg/220px-Southampton_Medieval_Merchants_House_Hall.jpg" width="220" height="164" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e6/Southampton_Medieval_Merchants_House_Hall.jpg/330px-Southampton_Medieval_Merchants_House_Hall.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e6/Southampton_Medieval_Merchants_House_Hall.jpg/440px-Southampton_Medieval_Merchants_House_Hall.jpg 2x" data-file-width="2592" data-file-height="1936" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Southampton_Medieval_Merchants_House_Hall.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The central hall of a <a href="/wiki/Medieval_Merchant%27s_House" title="Medieval Merchant&#39;s House">restored thirteenth-century house</a>, originally built with the profits from European trade</div></div></div>\n<p>The English economy was fundamentally <a href="/wiki/Economics_of_English_Agriculture_in_the_Middle_Ages" class="mw-redirect" title="Economics of English Agriculture in the Middle Ages">agricultural</a>, depending on growing crops such as <a href="/wiki/Wheat" title="Wheat">wheat</a>, <a href="/wiki/Barley" title="Barley">barley</a> and <a href="/wiki/Oats" class="mw-redirect" title="Oats">oats</a> on an <a href="/wiki/Open_field_system" class="mw-redirect" title="Open field system">open field system</a>, and husbanding <a href="/wiki/Sheep" title="Sheep">sheep</a>, <a href="/wiki/Cattle" title="Cattle">cattle</a> and <a href="/wiki/Pig" title="Pig">pigs</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-192" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-192">&#91;191&#93;</a></sup> Agricultural land was typically organised around <a href="/wiki/Manor" title="Manor">manors</a>, and was divided between some fields that the landowner would manage directly, called <a href="/wiki/Demesne" title="Demesne">demesne</a> land, and the majority of the fields that would be cultivated by local peasants.<sup id="cite_ref-Bartlett,_p._313_193-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Bartlett,_p._313-193">&#91;192&#93;</a></sup> These peasants would pay rent to the landowner either through agricultural labour on the lord\'s demesne fields or through rent in the form of cash and produce.<sup id="cite_ref-Bartlett,_p._313_193-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Bartlett,_p._313-193">&#91;192&#93;</a></sup> By the eleventh century a <a href="/wiki/Market_economy" title="Market economy">market economy</a> was flourishing across much of England, while the eastern and southern towns were heavily involved in international trade.<sup id="cite_ref-194" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-194">&#91;193&#93;</a></sup> Around 6,000 <a href="/wiki/Watermill" title="Watermill">watermills</a> were built to grind flour, freeing up labour for other more productive agricultural tasks.<sup id="cite_ref-195" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-195">&#91;194&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Economic growth began to falter at the end of the thirteenth century, owing to a combination of <a href="/wiki/Human_overpopulation" title="Human overpopulation">overpopulation</a>, land shortages and <a href="/wiki/Land_degradation" title="Land degradation">depleted soils</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-JordanBaileyAberthCantorJordan_196-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-JordanBaileyAberthCantorJordan-196">&#91;195&#93;</a></sup> The Great Famine shook the English economy severely and population growth ceased; the first outbreak of the Black Death in 1348 then killed around half the English population.<sup id="cite_ref-JordanBaileyAberthCantorJordan_196-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-JordanBaileyAberthCantorJordan-196">&#91;195&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p><a href="/wiki/Rodney_Hilton" title="Rodney Hilton">Rodney Hilton</a> and other scholars have argued that those peasants who survived famine, plague and disease, found their situation to be much improved. The period 1350-1450 was for them a golden age of prosperity and new opportunities. Land was plentiful, wages high, and serfdom had all but disappeared. It was possible to move about and rise higher in life. Younger sons and women especially benefited.<sup id="cite_ref-197" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-197">&#91;196&#93;</a></sup> As population growth resumed, however, the peasants again faced deprivation and famine.<sup id="cite_ref-198" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-198">&#91;197&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-199" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-199">&#91;198&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Conditions were less favourable for the great landowners. The agricultural sector shrank rapidly, with higher wages, lower prices and diminishing profits leading to the final demise of the old <a href="/wiki/Demesne" title="Demesne">demesne</a> system and the advent of the modern farming system centring on the charging of cash rents for lands.<sup id="cite_ref-200" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-200">&#91;199&#93;</a></sup> As returns on land fell, many estates, and in some cases entire settlements, were simply abandoned, and nearly 1,500 <a href="/wiki/Deserted_medieval_village" class="mw-redirect" title="Deserted medieval village">villages were deserted</a> during this period.<sup id="cite_ref-HodgettP206_201-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-HodgettP206-201">&#91;200&#93;</a></sup> A new class of gentry emerged who rented farms from the major nobility.<sup id="cite_ref-HodgettRamsay_202-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-HodgettRamsay-202">&#91;201&#93;</a></sup> Attempts were made by the government to regulate wages and consumption, but these largely collapsed in the decades following the Peasants\' Revolt of 1381.<sup id="cite_ref-203" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-203">&#91;202&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>The English <a href="/wiki/Cloth" class="mw-redirect" title="Cloth">cloth</a> industry grew at the start of the fifteenth century, and a new class of international English merchants emerged, typically based in London or the South-West, prospering at the expense of the older, shrinking economies of the eastern towns.<sup id="cite_ref-HodgettRamsay_202-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-HodgettRamsay-202">&#91;201&#93;</a></sup> These new trading systems brought about the end of many of the international fairs and the rise of the <a href="/wiki/Livery_Company" class="mw-redirect" title="Livery Company">chartered company</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-204" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-204">&#91;203&#93;</a></sup> Fishing in the North Sea expanded into deeper waters, backed by commercial investment from major merchants.<sup id="cite_ref-205" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-205">&#91;204&#93;</a></sup> Between 1440 and 1480, however, Europe entered a recession and England suffered the <a href="/wiki/Great_Slump_(15th_century)" title="Great Slump (15th century)">Great Slump</a>: trade collapsed, driving down agricultural prices, rents and ultimately the acceptable levels of royal taxation.<sup id="cite_ref-206" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-206">&#91;205&#93;</a></sup> The resulting tensions and discontent played an important part in Jack Cade\'s popular uprising in 1450 and the subsequent Wars of the Roses.<sup id="cite_ref-Hicks,_pp._52-53_163-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Hicks,_pp._52-53-163">&#91;162&#93;</a></sup> By the end of Middle Ages the economy had begun to recover and considerable improvements were being made in <a href="/wiki/Metalworking" title="Metalworking">metalworking</a> and <a href="/wiki/Shipbuilding" title="Shipbuilding">shipbuilding</a> that would shape the early
