modern economy.<sup id="cite_ref-207" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-207">&#91;206&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Technology_and_science">Technology and science</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=England_in_the_Late_Middle_Ages&amp;action=edit&amp;section=43" title="Edit section: Technology and science">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Science_in_the_Middle_Ages" class="mw-redirect" title="Science in the Middle Ages">Science in the Middle Ages</a> and <a href="/wiki/Medieval_technology" title="Medieval technology">Medieval technology</a></div>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Rievaulx_Abbey_2.jpg" class="image"><img alt="A photograph of a sandstone carving, broken into two pieces; on the left is the front half of a donkey, in the middle a fat man with a stick and a whip whilst on the right is a stylised windmill." src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ef/Rievaulx_Abbey_2.jpg/220px-Rievaulx_Abbey_2.jpg" width="220" height="165" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ef/Rievaulx_Abbey_2.jpg/330px-Rievaulx_Abbey_2.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ef/Rievaulx_Abbey_2.jpg/440px-Rievaulx_Abbey_2.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1600" data-file-height="1200" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Rievaulx_Abbey_2.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>A medieval carving from <a href="/wiki/Rievaulx_Abbey" title="Rievaulx Abbey">Rievaulx Abbey</a> showing one of the many new <a href="/wiki/Windmill" title="Windmill">windmills</a> established during the thirteenth century</div></div></div>\n<p>Technology and science in England advanced considerably during the Middle Ages, driven in part by the <a href="/wiki/Ancient_Greek_philosophy" title="Ancient Greek philosophy">Greek</a> and <a href="/wiki/Science_in_the_medieval_Islamic_world" title="Science in the medieval Islamic world">Islamic</a> thinking that reached England from the twelfth century.<sup id="cite_ref-DazigerGillingham2003p237_208-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-DazigerGillingham2003p237-208">&#91;207&#93;</a></sup> Many advances were made in scientific ideas, including the introduction of <a href="/wiki/Arabic_numerals" title="Arabic numerals">Arabic numerals</a> and a sequence of improvements in the <a href="/wiki/Unit_of_time" title="Unit of time">units used for measuring time</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-209" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-209">&#91;208&#93;</a></sup> Clocks were first built in England in the late thirteenth century, and the first <a href="/wiki/Clock#History_of_clocks" title="Clock">mechanical clocks</a> were being installed in cathedrals and abbeys by the 1320s.<sup id="cite_ref-210" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-210">&#91;209&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Astrology" title="Astrology">Astrology</a>, <a href="/wiki/Magic_(paranormal)" class="mw-redirect" title="Magic (paranormal)">magic</a> and <a href="/wiki/Palm_reading" class="mw-redirect" title="Palm reading">palm reading</a> were also considered important forms of knowledge in medieval England, although some doubted their reliability.<sup id="cite_ref-211" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-211">&#91;210&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>The period produced some influential English scholars. <a href="/wiki/Roger_Bacon" title="Roger Bacon">Roger Bacon</a> (c. 1214\xe2\x80\x9394), a philosopher and Franciscan friar, produced works on <a href="/wiki/Natural_philosophy" title="Natural philosophy">natural philosophy</a>, <a href="/wiki/Astronomy" title="Astronomy">astronomy</a> and <a href="/wiki/Alchemy" title="Alchemy">alchemy</a>; his work set out the theoretical basis for future <a href="/wiki/Scientific_method" title="Scientific method">experimentation</a> in the natural sciences.<sup id="cite_ref-212" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-212">&#91;211&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/William_of_Ockham" title="William of Ockham">William of Ockham</a> (c. 1287\xe2\x80\x931347) helped to fuse Latin, Greek and Islamic writing into a general theory of logic; "<a href="/wiki/Ockham%27s_Razor" class="mw-redirect" title="Ockham&#39;s Razor">Ockham\'s Razor</a>" was one of his oft-cited conclusions.<sup id="cite_ref-213" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-213">&#91;212&#93;</a></sup> Despite the limitations of <a href="/wiki/Medieval_medicine" class="mw-redirect" title="Medieval medicine">medieval medicine</a>, <a href="/wiki/Gilbertus_Anglicus" title="Gilbertus Anglicus">Gilbertus Anglicus</a> published the <i>Compendium Medicinae</i>, one of the longest medical works ever written in Latin.<sup id="cite_ref-214" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-214">&#91;213&#93;</a></sup> Prominent historical and science texts began to be translated into English for the first time in the second half of the fourteenth century, including the <i><a href="/wiki/Polychronicon_of_Ranulf_Higden" class="mw-redirect" title="Polychronicon of Ranulf Higden">Polychronicon</a></i> and <i><a href="/wiki/The_Travels_of_Sir_John_Mandeville" class="mw-redirect" title="The Travels of Sir John Mandeville">The Travels of Sir John Mandeville</a></i>.<sup id="cite_ref-215" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-215">&#91;214&#93;</a></sup> The universities of Oxford and <a href="/wiki/Cambridge_University" class="mw-redirect" title="Cambridge University">Cambridge</a> were established during the eleventh and twelfth centuries, drawing on the model of the <a href="/wiki/University_of_Paris" title="University of Paris">University of Paris</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-216" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-216">&#91;215&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Technological advances proceeded in a range of areas. Watermills to grind grain had existed during most of the Anglo-Saxon period, using <a href="/wiki/Watermill#Operation" title="Watermill">horizontal mill</a> designs; from the twelfth century on many more were built, eliminating the use of hand mills, with the older horizontal mills gradually supplanted by a new <a href="/wiki/Watermill#Operation" title="Watermill">vertical mill</a> design.<sup id="cite_ref-217" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-217">&#91;216&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Windmill" title="Windmill">Windmills</a> began to be built in the late twelfth century and slowly became more common.<sup id="cite_ref-218" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-218">&#91;217&#93;</a></sup> Water-powered <a href="/wiki/Fulling_mill" class="mw-redirect" title="Fulling mill">fulling mills</a> and <a href="/wiki/Power_hammer" title="Power hammer">powered hammers</a> first appeared in the twelfth century; water power was harnessed to assist in <a href="/wiki/Smelting" title="Smelting">smelting</a> by the fourteenth century, with the first <a href="/wiki/Blast_furnace" title="Blast furnace">blast furnace</a> opening in 1496.<sup id="cite_ref-219" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-219">&#91;218&#93;</a></sup> New mining methods were developed and horse-powered pumps were installed in English mines by the end of the Middle Ages.<sup id="cite_ref-220" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-220">&#91;219&#93;</a></sup> The introduction of <a href="/wiki/Hops" title="Hops">hopped</a> <a href="/wiki/Beer" title="Beer">beer</a> transformed the brewing industry in the fourteenth century, and new techniques were invented to better preserve fish.<sup id="cite_ref-221" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-221">&#91;220&#93;</a></sup> Glazed pottery became widespread in the twelfth and thirteenth centuries, with <a href="/wiki/Stoneware" title="Stoneware">stoneware</a> pots largely replacing wooden plates and bowls by the fifteenth century.<sup id="cite_ref-222" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-222">&#91;221&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/William_Caxton" title="William Caxton">William Caxton</a> and <a href="/wiki/Wynkyn_de_Worde" title="Wynkyn de Worde">Wynkyn de Worde</a> began using the <a href="/wiki/Printing_press" title="Printing press">printing press</a> during the late fifteenth century.<sup id="cite_ref-223" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-223">&#91;222&#93;</a></sup> Transport links were also improved; many road bridges were either erected or rebuilt in stone during the long economic boom of the twelfth and thirteenth centuries. England\'s maritime trade benefited from the introduction of <a href="/wiki/Cog_(ship)" title="Cog (ship)">cog ships</a>, and many docks were improved and fitted with cranes for the first time.<sup id="cite_ref-224" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-224">&#91;223&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Warfare">Warfare</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=England_in_the_Late_Middle_Ages&amp;action=edit&amp;section=44" title="Edit section: Warfare">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Armies">Armies</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=England_in_the_Late_Middle_Ages&amp;action=edit&amp;section=45" title="Edit section: Armies">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Medieval_warfare" title="Medieval warfare">Medieval warfare</a></div>\n<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Coventry_Sallet_HAGAM.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d2/Coventry_Sallet_HAGAM.jpg/220px-Coventry_Sallet_HAGAM.jpg" width="220" height="193" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d2/Coventry_Sallet_HAGAM.jpg/330px-Coventry_Sallet_HAGAM.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d2/Coventry_Sallet_HAGAM.jpg/440px-Coventry_Sallet_HAGAM.jpg 2x" data-file-width="966" data-file-height="846" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Coventry_Sallet_HAGAM.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The fifteenth-century <a href="/wiki/Coventry_Sallet" title="Coventry Sallet">Coventry Sallet</a></div></div></div>\n<p>In the late thirteenth century Edward I expanded the <i>familia regis</i>, the permanent military household of the king, which was supported in war by <a href="/wiki/Feudal" class="mw-redirect" title="Feudal">feudal</a> levies, drawn up by local nobles for a limited period of service during a campaign.<sup id="cite_ref-225" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-225">&#91;224&#93;</a></sup> to become a small standing army. It formed the core of much larger armies up to 28,700 strong, largely comprising foot soldiers, for campaigns in Scotland and France.<sup id="cite_ref-226" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-226">&#91;225&#93;</a></sup> By the time of Edward III, armies were smaller in size, but the troops were typically better equipped and uniformed, and the archers carried the <a href="/wiki/Longbow" title="Longbow">longbow</a>, a potentially devastating weapon.<sup id="cite_ref-227" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-227">&#91;226&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Cannon" title="Cannon">Cannon</a> were first used by English forces at battles such as <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Cr%C3%A9cy" title="Battle of Cr\xc3\xa9cy">Cr\xc3\xa9cy</a> in 1346.<sup id="cite_ref-228" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-228">&#91;227&#93;</a></sup> Soldiers began to be contracted for specific campaigns, a practice which may have hastened the development of the armies of retainers that grew up under bastard feudalism.<sup id="cite_ref-229" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-229">&#91;228&#93;</a></sup> By the late fifteenth century, however, English armies were somewhat backward by wider European standards; the Wars of the Roses were fought by inexperienced soldiers, often with outdated weapons, allowing the European forces which intervened in the conflict to have a decisive effect on the outcomes of battles.<sup id="cite_ref-230" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-230">&#91;229&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Navies">Navies</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=England_in_the_Late_Middle_Ages&amp;action=edit&amp;section=46" title="Edit section: Navies">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Medieval_naval_warfare" class="mw-redirect" title="Medieval naval warfare">Medieval naval warfare</a></div>\n<p>English fleets in the thirteenth and fourteenth centuries typically comprised specialist vessels, such as <a href="/wiki/Galley" title="Galley">galleys</a> and large transport ships, and <a href="/wiki/Impressment" title="Impressment">pressed</a> merchant vessels conscripted into action; the latter increasingly included <a href="/wiki/Cog_(ship)" title="Cog (ship)">cogs</a>, a new form of sailing ship.<sup id="cite_ref-231" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-231">&#91;230&#93;</a></sup> Battles might be fought when one fleet found another at anchor, such as the English <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Sluys" title="Battle of Sluys">victory at Sluys</a> in 1340, or in more open waters, as <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Les_Espagnols_sur_Mer" class="mw-redirect" title="Battle of Les Espagnols sur Mer">off the coast of Winchelsea</a> in 1350; raiding campaigns, such as the <a href="/wiki/English_Channel_naval_campaign_(1338%E2%80%931339)" class="mw-redirect" title="English Channel naval campaign (1338\xe2\x80\x931339)">French attacks</a> on the south of England between 1338\xe2\x80\x9339, could cause devastation from which some towns never fully recovered.<sup id="cite_ref-232" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-232">&#91;231&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Fortifications">Fortifications</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=England_in_the_Late_Middle_Ages&amp;action=edit&amp;section=47" title="Edit section: Fortifications">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Castles_in_Great_Britain_and_Ireland" title="Castles in Great Britain and Ireland">Castles in Great Britain and Ireland</a> and <a href="/wiki/List_of_town_walls_in_England_and_Wales" title="List of town walls in England and Wales">List of town walls in England and Wales</a></div>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:RidsdalePanorma.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0e/RidsdalePanorma.jpg/220px-RidsdalePanorma.jpg" width="220" height="98" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0e/RidsdalePanorma.jpg/330px-RidsdalePanorma.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0e/RidsdalePanorma.jpg/440px-RidsdalePanorma.jpg 2x" data-file-width="737" data-file-height="329" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:RidsdalePanorma.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>A reconstruction of the city of York in the fifteenth century, showing the <a href="/wiki/York_city_walls" title="York city walls">city walls</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Baile_Hill,_York" class="mw-redirect" title="Baile Hill, York">Old Baile</a> (left) and <a href="/wiki/York_Castle" title="York Castle">York Castle</a> (right)</div></div></div>\n<p>During the twelfth century the Normans had begun to build more castles in stone, with characteristic square <a href="/wiki/Keep" title="Keep">keeps</a> that supported both military and political functions.<sup id="cite_ref-233" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-233">&#91;232&#93;</a></sup> Royal castles were used to control key towns and forests, whilst <a href="/wiki/Baron" title="Baron">baronial</a> castles were used by the Norman lords to control their widespread estates; a feudal system called the castle-guard was sometimes used to provide garrisons.<sup id="cite_ref-234" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-234">&#91;233&#93;</a></sup> Castles and <a href="/wiki/Siege" title="Siege">sieges</a> continued to grow in military sophistication during the twelfth century, and in the thirteenth century new defensive town walls were constructed across England.<sup id="cite_ref-235" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-235">&#91;234&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>By the fourteenth century, castles were combining defences with luxurious, sophisticated living arrangements and landscaped gardens and parks.<sup id="cite_ref-236" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-236">&#91;235&#93;</a></sup> Early gunpowder weapons were used to defend castles by the end of the fourteenth century and <a href="/wiki/Gunport" class="mw-redirect" title="Gunport">gunports</a> became an essential feature for a fashionable castle.<sup id="cite_ref-237" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-237">&#91;236&#93;</a></sup> The economics of maintaining castles meant that many were left to decline or abandoned; in contrast, a small number of castles were developed by the very wealthy into palaces that hosted lavish feasts and celebrations amid elaborate architecture.<sup id="cite_ref-238" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-238">&#91;237&#93;</a></sup> Smaller defensible structures called tower houses emerged in the north of England to protect against the Scottish threat.<sup id="cite_ref-239" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-239">&#91;238&#93;</a></sup> By the late medieval period, town walls were increasingly less military in character and more often expressions of civic pride or part of urban governance: many grand gatehouses were built in the fourteenth and fifteenth centuries for these purposes.<sup id="cite_ref-240" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-240">&#91;239&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Arts">Arts</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=England_in_the_Late_Middle_Ages&amp;action=edit&amp;section=48" title="Edit section: Arts">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Art">Art</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=England_in_the_Late_Middle_Ages&amp;action=edit&amp;section=49" title="Edit section: Art">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Medieval_art" title="Medieval art">Medieval art</a></div>\n<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Ellesmere_Manuscript_Knight_Portrait.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e0/Ellesmere_Manuscript_Knight_Portrait.jpg/170px-Ellesmere_Manuscript_Knight_Portrait.jpg" width="170" height="249" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e0/Ellesmere_Manuscript_Knight_Portrait.jpg/255px-Ellesmere_Manuscript_Knight_Portrait.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e0/Ellesmere_Manuscript_Knight_Portrait.jpg/340px-Ellesmere_Manuscript_Knight_Portrait.jpg 2x" data-file-width="590" data-file-height="864" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Ellesmere_Manuscript_Knight_Portrait.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The <a href="/wiki/Ellesmere_Chaucer" title="Ellesmere Chaucer">Ellesmere</a> <a href="/wiki/Illuminated_manuscript" title="Illuminated manuscript">illuminated manuscript</a> of <a href="/wiki/The_Canterbury_Tales" title="The Canterbury Tales">the Canterbury Tales</a> by <a href="/wiki/Geoffrey_Chaucer" title="Geoffrey Chaucer">Geoffrey Chaucer</a>, early fifteenth-century, showing <a href="/wiki/The_Knight%27s_Tale" title="The Knight&#39;s Tale">the Knight</a> (right)</div></div></div>\n<p>Medieval England produced art in the form of paintings, carvings, books, fabrics and many functional but beautiful objects. A wide range of materials were used, including gold, glass and ivory, the art usually drawing overt attention to the materials utilised in the designs.<sup id="cite_ref-241" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-241">&#91;240&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Stained_glass" title="Stained glass">Stained glass</a> became a common form of English art during this period, although the coloured glass for these works was almost entirely imported from Europe.<sup id="cite_ref-242" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-242">&#91;241&#93;</a></sup> Little early stained glass in England has survived, but it typically had both an ornamental and educational function, while later works also commemorated the sponsors of the windows into the designs.<sup id="cite_ref-243" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-243">&#91;242&#93;</a></sup> English embroidery in the early fourteenth century was of an especially high quality, and revived its international reputation from <a href="/wiki/Anglo-Saxon_art#Textile_art" title="Anglo-Saxon art">Anglo-Saxon times</a>; works produced by nuns and London professionals were exported across Europe, known as <i><a href="/wiki/Opus_anglicanum" class="mw-redirect" title="Opus anglicanum">opus anglicanum</a></i>.<sup id="cite_ref-244" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-244">&#91;243&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>During the period manuscript painting in England became again the equal of any in Europe, in <a href="/wiki/Romanesque_art" title="Romanesque art">Romanesque</a> works like the <a href="/wiki/Winchester_Bible" title="Winchester Bible">Winchester Bible</a> and the <a href="/wiki/St_Albans_Psalter" class="mw-redirect" title="St Albans Psalter">St Albans Psalter</a>, and then early Gothic ones like the <a href="/wiki/Queen_Mary_Psalter" title="Queen Mary Psalter">Queen Mary Psalter</a> and <a href="/wiki/Tickhill_Psalter" title="Tickhill Psalter">Tickhill Psalter</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-245" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-245">&#91;244&#93;</a></sup> English illumination fell away in the final phase of the Gothic period as elite patrons began instead to commission works from Paris and then Flanders. Competition from a strong French industry also eclipsed English ivory-carving.<sup id="cite_ref-246" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-246">&#91;245&#93;</a></sup> Some of the extremely rare survivals of English medieval <a href="/wiki/Panel_painting" title="Panel painting">panel paintings</a>, like the <a href="/wiki/Westminster_Retable" title="Westminster Retable">Westminster Retable</a> and <a href="/wiki/Wilton_Diptych" title="Wilton Diptych">Wilton Diptych</a> (the artist\'s nationality here is uncertain), are of the highest quality.<sup id="cite_ref-247" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-247">&#91;246&#93;</a></sup> Wall-paintings were very common in both churches and palaces, but now very little survives.<sup id="cite_ref-248" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-248">&#91;247&#93;</a></sup> There was an industry producing <a href="/wiki/Nottingham_alabaster" title="Nottingham alabaster">Nottingham alabaster</a> reliefs, made in Nottingham, but also <a href="/wiki/Burton-on-Trent" class="mw-redirect" title="Burton-on-Trent">Burton-on-Trent</a>, Coventry, and perhaps Lincoln and London, ranging from commissioned <a href="/wiki/Altarpiece" title="Altarpiece">altarpieces</a> to single figures and narrative panels, which were exported across Europe.<sup id="cite_ref-249" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-249">&#91;248&#93;</a></sup> English monumental carving in stone and wood was often of very high quality, and much of it survived the <a href="/wiki/English_Reformation" title="English Reformation">Reformation</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-250" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-250">&#91;249&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span id="Literature.2C_drama_and_music"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Literature,_drama_and_music">Literature, drama and music</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=England_in_the_Late_Middle_Ages&amp;action=edit&amp;section=50" title="Edit section: Literature, drama and music">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Middle_English_literature" title="Middle English literature">Middle English literature</a>, <a href="/wiki/Music_in_Medieval_England" title="Music in Medieval England">Music in Medieval England</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Medieval_theatre" title="Medieval theatre">Medieval theatre</a></div>\n<p>Poetry and stories written in French were popular after the Norman conquest, and by the twelfth century some works on English history began to be produced in French verse.<sup id="cite_ref-251" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-251">&#91;250&#93;</a></sup> Romantic poems about tournaments and courtly love became popular in Paris and this fashion spread into England in the form of <i>lays</i>; stories about the court of <a href="/wiki/King_Arthur" title="King Arthur">King Arthur</a> were also fashionable, due in part to the interest of Henry II.<sup id="cite_ref-252" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-252">&#91;251&#93;</a></sup> English continued to be used on a modest scale to write local religious works and some poems in the north of England, but most major works were produced in Latin or French.<sup id="cite_ref-253" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-253">&#91;252&#93;</a></sup> Among others the <a href="/wiki/Pearl_Poet" title="Pearl Poet">Pearl Poet</a>, <a href="/wiki/John_Gower" title="John Gower">John Gower</a> and <a href="/wiki/William_Langland" title="William Langland">William Langland</a> created a distinctive <a href="/wiki/English_culture" class="mw-redirect" title="English culture">English culture</a> and <a href="/wiki/English_art" title="English art">art</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Jones2012_81-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Jones2012-81">&#91;81&#93;</a></sup> In the reign of Richard II there was an upsurge in the use of <a href="/wiki/Middle_English" title="Middle English">Middle English</a> in poetry, sometimes termed "Ricardian poetry", although the works still emulated French fashions.<sup id="cite_ref-254" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-254">&#91;253&#93;</a></sup> The work of <a href="/wiki/Geoffrey_Chaucer" title="Geoffrey Chaucer">Geoffrey Chaucer</a> from the 1370s, however, culminating in the influential <i><a href="/wiki/Canterbury_Tales" class="mw-redirect" title="Canterbury Tales">Canterbury Tales</a></i>, was uniquely English in style.<sup id="cite_ref-255" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-255">&#91;254&#93;</a></sup> Major pieces of courtly poetry continued to be produced into the fifteenth century by Chaucher\'s disciples, and <a href="/wiki/Thomas_Malory" title="Thomas Malory">Thomas Malory</a> compiled the older Arthurian tales to produce <i><a href="/wiki/Le_Morte_d%27Arthur" title="Le Morte d&#39;Arthur">Le Morte d\'Arthur</a></i>.<sup id="cite_ref-256" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-256">&#91;255&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Music and singing were important in England during the medieval period, being used in religious ceremonies, court occasions and to accompany theatrical works.<sup id="cite_ref-257" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-257">&#91;256&#93;</a></sup> Singing techniques called <a href="/wiki/Gymel" title="Gymel">gymel</a> were introduced in England in the thirteenth century, accompanied by instruments such as the <a href="/wiki/Guitar" title="Guitar">guitar</a>, <a href="/wiki/Harp" title="Harp">harp</a>, <a href="/wiki/Pipe_(instrument)" title="Pipe (instrument)">pipes</a> and <a href="/wiki/Organ_(music)" title="Organ (music)">organ</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-258" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-258">&#91;257&#93;</a></sup> Henry IV sponsored an extensive range of music in England, while his son Henry V brought back many influences from occupied France.<sup id="cite_ref-259" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-259">&#91;258&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Carol_(music)" title="Carol (music)">Carols</a> became an important form of music in the fifteenth century; originally these had been a song sung during a dance with a prominent refrain \xe2\x80\x94 the fifteenth century form lost the dancing and introduced strong religious overtones.<sup id="cite_ref-260" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-260">&#91;259&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Ballad" title="Ballad">Ballads</a> were also popular from the late fourteenth century, including the <i><a href="/wiki/Ballad_of_Chevy_Chase" class="mw-redirect" title="Ballad of Chevy Chase">Ballad of Chevy Chase</a></i> and others describing the activities of <a href="/wiki/Robin_Hood" title="Robin Hood">Robin Hood</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-261" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-261">&#91;260&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Miracle_play" class="mw-redirect" title="Miracle play">Miracle plays</a> were performed to communicate the Bible in various locations. By the late fourteenth century, these had been extended into vernacular mystery plays which performed annually over several days, broken up into various cycles of plays; a handful have survived into the twenty-first century.<sup id="cite_ref-262" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-262">&#91;261&#93;</a></sup> Guilds competed to produce the best plays in each town and performances were often an expression of civic identity.<sup id="cite_ref-263" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-263">&#91;262&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Architecture">Architecture</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=England_in_the_Late_Middle_Ages&amp;action=edit&amp;section=51" title="Edit section: Architecture">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/English_Gothic_architecture" title="English Gothic architecture">English Gothic architecture</a></div>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a href="/wiki/File:YorkMinsterWest.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3f/YorkMinsterWest.jpg/170px-YorkMinsterWest.jpg" width="170" height="227" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3f/YorkMinsterWest.jpg/255px-YorkMinsterWest.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3f/YorkMinsterWest.jpg/340px-YorkMinsterWest.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1200" data-file-height="1600" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:YorkMinsterWest.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The west front of <a href="/wiki/York_Minster" title="York Minster">York Minster</a> is a fine example of Decorated Gothic</div></div></div>\n<p>During the twelfth century the Anglo-Norman style became richer and more ornate, with pointed arches derived from French architecture replacing the curved Romanesque designs; this style is termed <a href="/wiki/English_Gothic_architecture" title="English Gothic architecture">Early English Gothic</a> and continued, with variation, throughout the rest of the Middle Ages.<sup id="cite_ref-264" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-264">&#91;263&#93;</a></sup> In the early fourteenth century the <a href="/wiki/English_Gothic_architecture#Perpendicular_Gothic" title="English Gothic architecture">Perpendicular Gothic</a> style was created in England, with an emphasis on verticality, immense windows and soaring arcades.<sup id="cite_ref-265" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-265">&#91;264&#93;</a></sup> Fine timber roofs in a variety of styles, but in particular the <a href="/wiki/Hammerbeam_roof" title="Hammerbeam roof">hammerbeam</a>, were built in many English buildings.<sup id="cite_ref-Myers,_p._105_266-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Myers,_p._105-266">&#91;265&#93;</a></sup> In the fifteenth century the architectural focus turned away from cathedrals and monasteries in favour of parish churches, often decorated with richly carved woodwork; in turn, these churches influenced the design of new <a href="/wiki/Chantry" title="Chantry">chantry</a> chapels for existing cathedrals.<sup id="cite_ref-267" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-267">&#91;266&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>By the fourteenth century grander houses and castles were sophisticated affairs: expensively tiled, often featuring murals and glass windows, these buildings were often designed as a set of apartments to allow greater privacy.<sup id="cite_ref-268" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-268">&#91;267&#93;</a></sup> Fashionable brick began to be used in some parts of the country, copying French tastes.<sup id="cite_ref-Myers,_p._105_266-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Myers,_p._105-266">&#91;265&#93;</a></sup> Architecture that emulated the older defensive designs remained popular.<sup id="cite_ref-269" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-269">&#91;268&#93;</a></sup> Less is known about the houses of peasants during this period, although many peasants appear to have lived in relatively substantial, timber-framed long-houses; the quality of these houses improved in the prosperous years following the Black Death, often being built by professional craftsmen.<sup id="cite_ref-270" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-270">&#91;269&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Legacy">Legacy</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=England_in_the_Late_Middle_Ages&amp;action=edit&amp;section=52" title="Edit section: Legacy">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Historiography">Historiography</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=England_in_the_Late_Middle_Ages&amp;action=edit&amp;section=53" title="Edit section: Historiography">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<p>In the sixteenth century, the first academic histories began to be written, typically drawing primarily on the chroniclers and interpreting them in the light of current political concerns.<sup id="cite_ref-271" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-271">&#91;270&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Edward_Gibbon" title="Edward Gibbon">Edward Gibbon</a>\'s eighteenth-century writings were influential, presenting the medieval period as a dark age between the glories of Rome and the rebirth of civilisation in the Early Modern period.<sup id="cite_ref-272" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-272">&#91;271&#93;</a></sup> Late Victorian historians continued to use the chroniclers as sources, but also deployed documents such as <i>Domesday Book</i> and <i>Magna Carta</i>, alongside newly discovered financial, legal and commercial records. They produced a <a href="/wiki/Progress_(history)" class="mw-redirect" title="Progress (history)">progressive</a> account of political and economic development in England.<sup id="cite_ref-273" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-273">&#91;272&#93;</a></sup> The growth of the <a href="/wiki/British_Empire" title="British Empire">British Empire</a> spurred interest in the various periods of English hegemony during the Middle Ages, including the Angevin Empire and the Hundred Years\' War.<sup id="cite_ref-274" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-274">&#91;273&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Battle_of_Tewkesbury_reenactment_-_pre-clash_preparation.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/80/Battle_of_Tewkesbury_reenactment_-_pre-clash_preparation.jpg/220px-Battle_of_Tewkesbury_reenactment_-_pre-clash_preparation.jpg" width="220" height="167" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/80/Battle_of_Tewkesbury_reenactment_-_pre-clash_preparation.jpg/330px-Battle_of_Tewkesbury_reenactment_-_pre-clash_preparation.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/80/Battle_of_Tewkesbury_reenactment_-_pre-clash_preparation.jpg/440px-Battle_of_Tewkesbury_reenactment_-_pre-clash_preparation.jpg 2x" data-file-width="2415" data-file-height="1833" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Battle_of_Tewkesbury_reenactment_-_pre-clash_preparation.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Historical_re-enactment" class="mw-redirect" title="Historical re-enactment">Re-enactments</a> of English medieval events, such as the <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Tewkesbury" title="Battle of Tewkesbury">battle of Tewkesbury</a> shown here, form part of the modern <a href="/wiki/Cultural_heritage" title="Cultural heritage">heritage industry</a></div></div></div>\n<p>By the 1930s, older historical analyses were challenged by a range of <a href="/wiki/Neopositivism" class="mw-redirect" title="Neopositivism">neo-positivist</a>, <a href="/wiki/Marxist" class="mw-redirect" title="Marxist">Marxist</a> and <a href="/wiki/Econometric" class="mw-redirect" title="Econometric">econometric</a> approaches, supported by a widening body of documentary, archaeological and scientific evidence.<sup id="cite_ref-275" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-275">&#91;274&#93;</a></sup> Marxist and <a href="/wiki/Neo-Marxist" class="mw-redirect" title="Neo-Marxist">Neo-Marxist</a> analyses continued to be popular in the post-war years, producing seminal works on economic issues and social protests.<sup id="cite_ref-276" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-276">&#91;275&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Post-modern" class="mw-redirect" title="Post-modern">Post-modern</a> analysis became influential in the 1970s and 1980s, focusing on identity, gender, interpretation and culture. Many studies focused on particular regions or groups, drawing on new records and new scientific approaches, including <a href="/wiki/Landscape_archaeology" title="Landscape archaeology">landscape</a> and <a href="/wiki/Environmental_archaeology" title="Environmental archaeology">environmental archaeology</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-277" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-277">&#91;276&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Popular_representations">Popular representations</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=England_in_the_Late_Middle_Ages&amp;action=edit&amp;section=54" title="Edit section: Popular representations">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Depiction_of_the_Middle_Ages_in_popular_culture" class="mw-redirect" title="Depiction of the Middle Ages in popular culture">Depiction of the Middle Ages in popular culture</a></div>\n<p>The period has also been used in a wide range of popular culture. <a href="/wiki/William_Shakespeare" title="William Shakespeare">William Shakespeare</a>\'s plays on the lives of the medieval kings have proved to have had long lasting appeal, heavily influencing both popular interpretations and histories of figures such as King John and Henry V.<sup id="cite_ref-278" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-278">&#91;277&#93;</a></sup> Other playwrights have since taken key medieval events, such as the death of Thomas Becket, and used them to draw out contemporary themes and issues.<sup id="cite_ref-279" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-279">&#91;278&#93;</a></sup> Medieval mystery plays continue to be enacted in key English towns and cities. Film-makers have drawn extensively on the medieval period, often taking themes from Shakespeare or the Robin Hood ballads for inspiration.<sup id="cite_ref-280" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-280">&#91;279&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Historical_fiction" title="Historical fiction">Historical fiction</a> set in England during the Middle Ages remains persistently popular, with the 1980s and 1990s seeing a particular growth of historical <a href="/wiki/Detective_fiction" title="Detective fiction">detective fiction</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-281" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-281">&#91;280&#93;</a></sup> The period has also inspired fantasy writers, including <a href="/wiki/J._R._R._Tolkien" title="J. R. R. Tolkien">J. R. R. Tolkien</a>\'s stories of <a href="/wiki/Middle-earth" title="Middle-earth">Middle-earth</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-282" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-282">&#91;281&#93;</a></sup> English medieval music was revived from the 1950s, with choral and musical groups attempting to authentically reproduce the original sounds.<sup id="cite_ref-283" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-283">&#91;282&#93;</a></sup> Medieval <a href="/wiki/Living_history" title="Living history">living history</a> events were first held during the nineteenth and early twentieth centuries, and the period has inspired a considerable community of <a href="/wiki/Historical_reenactment" title="Historical reenactment">historical re-enactors</a>, part of England\'s growing heritage industry.<sup id="cite_ref-284" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-284">&#91;283&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Notes">Notes</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=England_in_the_Late_Middle_Ages&amp;action=edit&amp;section=55" title="Edit section: Notes">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<div class="reflist" style="list-style-type: decimal;">\n<div class="mw-references-wrap"><ol class="references">\n<li id="cite_note-142"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-142">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">The utility of the term bastard feudalism has been extensively discussed by historians, with many different conclusions being drawn.<sup id="cite_ref-141" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-141">&#91;141&#93;</a></sup></span>\n</li>\n</ol></div></div>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="References">References</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=England_in_the_Late_Middle_Ages&amp;action=edit&amp;section=56" title="Edit section: References">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<div class="reflist columns references-column-width" style="-moz-column-width: 20em; pp.&#160;67, 71</span>\n</li>\n<li id="cite_note-McKisack19596-36"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-McKisack19596_36-0">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><a href="#cite_ref-Maddicott103_37-0">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><a href="#CITEREFMaddicott1970">Maddicott 1970</a>, p.&#160;103</span>\n</li>\n<li id="cite_note-McKisack195910-38"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-McKisack195910_38-0">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><a href="#CITEREFMcKisack1959">McKisack 1959</a>, p.&#160;10</span>\n</li>\n<li id="cite_note-39"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-39">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><a href="#CITEREFJordan1997">Jordan 1997</a>, pp.&#160;37 and 57</span>\n</li>\n<li id="cite_note-41"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-41">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><a
